The Principle of Rulings Requiring Qada in Dar al-Harb
Definition and expansion of a principle on judicial rulings in dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rulings that require a judge's decree (qada) and those that do not, and apply this distinction to scenarios in dar al-Islam and dar al-harb.

Core Idea
Some rulings need a judge to be enforced; others are effective immediately even in dar al-harb.

This lesson introduces a fundamental Hanafi principle that determines the legal capacity of Muslims in hostile territory (dar al-harb). Not all Islamic rulings require a court judgment; many are self-executing. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi fiqh to situations involving cross-border acts and the enforcement of penalties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Read the opening line: 'every ruling that does not require a judge's decree, dar al-Islam and dar al-harb are equal; every ruling that requires a judge's decree is not established by performing it in dar al-harb.' This is the core rule.
2
Distinguish the two categories
Separate rulings into: (1) those not requiring qada (e.g., contracts, worship obligations) and (2) those requiring qada (e.g., hadd penalties).
The first category is effective immediately; the second requires judicial confirmation after entering dar al-Islam.
3
Examine the examples
For the first category: sale, purchase, validity of seizure, emancipation, fasting, prayer. For the second: adultery (zina) leading to hadd. Note that these are illustrative, not exhaustive.
4
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Consider a Muslim in dar al-harb who commits theft (sariqa). Does the hadd penalty apply immediately? According to the principle, it requires qada, so it is not enforced until the thief enters dar al-Islam and a judge decrees it.
This shows the practical difference: the act is sinful but the legal penalty is deferred.
5
Understand the thamarah (practical consequence)
The distinction affects rulings like shuf'a (pre-emption): a sale in dar al-harb is valid for shuf'a if both parties are Muslim, but the right of pre-emption may only be exercisable upon entry into dar al-Islam? Not from the text, but it informs how jurists treat cross-border transactions.
The principle ensures that Muslims in dar al-harb are not entirely outside Islamic law; they remain obligated in many matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا أصل تنبني عليه هذه المسائل يجب العلم به وهو أن كل حكم لا يفتقر إلى قضاء القاضي فدار الإسلام ودار الحرب في ذلك الحكم على حد سواء، وكل حكم مفتقر إلى قضاء القاضي لا يثبت ذلك في حق من كان من المسلمين في دار الحرب بمباشرة ذلك الحكم في دار الحرب ؛ نظير الأول البيع والشراء وصحة الاستيلاء ونقوذ العتق ووجوب الصوم والصلاة فإن هذه الأحكام كلها من أحكام المسلمين وتجري على من كان في دار الحرب من المسلمين. ونظير الثاني الزنا فإن المسلم إذا زنى في دار الحرب ثم دخل دار الإسلام يقام عليه الحد .
Here there is a principle upon which these issues are built, and it must be known: that every ruling that does not require a judge's decree (qada), then dar al-Islam and dar al-harb are equal in that ruling; and every ruling that requires a judge's decree, it is not established for Muslims who are in dar al-harb by directly performing that ruling in dar al-harb. An example of the first is sale, purchase, validity of seizure, the effectiveness of emancipation, and the obligation of fasting and prayer, for all these are rulings of Muslims and apply to Muslims in dar al-harb. An example of the second is adultery: if a Muslim commits adultery in dar al-harb and then enters dar al-Islam, the hadd penalty is established upon him.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage lays out a fundamental principle of Hanafi fiqh regarding the legal capacity of Muslims in dar al-harb (hostile territory). The principle distinguishes between two types of rulings: (1) Those that do not require a judge's decree (qada), such as the validity of contracts, emancipation, and obligations like prayer and fasting. These are effective immediately for a Muslim in dar al-harb; they are bound to pray and fast, and their sales are valid. (2) Those that require a judge's decree, such as the hadd penalty for zina, can only be enforced once the Muslim enters dar al-Islam and the judge decrees it. The point is that Muslims in dar al-harb are still subject to Islamic law in matters that do not require judicial intervention. This principle explains why, for example, a Muslim's purchase of a house in dar al-harb (from a harbi?) may be valid for shuf'a purposes, but the shafee's right might be affected by the location. It is a key distinction in the jurisprudence of dar al-harb.

Key Terms
قضاء القاضي (qada al-qadi): A judge's decree or judicial decision; rulings that require an official court judgment.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory not under Islamic rule and not in a peace treaty; hostile territory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle, which of the following rulings requires a judge's decree (qada) to be enforced?
A) Validity of a sale
B) Obligation of fasting
C) Hadd penalty for zina
D) Effectiveness of emancipation

Key Takeaway
Rulings that do not require a judge's decree are effective immediately in dar al-harb; those that require a judge are only enforceable after entering dar al-Islam.

Preemptor's Options When Buyer Built or Planted
Intra-Hanafi dispute on forced removal of building/planting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on forcing removal of building/planting and apply the mufta bihi ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
The preemptor may force removal because his right is a confirmed right (haqq muta'akkid).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: when the buyer has built or planted on property subject to pre-emption, does the preemptor have the right to force removal? The Zahir al-Riwayah (Abu Hanifa) grants him that choice, while Abu Yusuf holds he cannot. Understanding this requires analyzing the nature of the pre-emption right and analogies to other areas of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its context
The passage concerns the preemptor's options after judgment in his favor, when the buyer has already built or planted on the land. The central question: can the preemptor force the buyer to remove these additions?
This is a procedural right (hukm wad'i), not a taklifi ruling.
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Zahir al-Riwayah (Abu Hanifa): preemptor has choice – take land with compensation for value of building/planting as if removed, or force removal and take land empty. Abu Yusuf: preemptor cannot force removal; may only take with compensation or leave.
Abu Yusuf's view is shared by Shafi'i and Malik.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's proof
Abu Yusuf argues by analogy to a donee or buyer in a fasid purchase, who are not forced to remove because they acted with permission. He also uses the harm principle: forcing removal harms the buyer more than compensating the preemptor.
The key difference: the buyer owns the property at time of building.
4
Analyze the Zahir al-Riwayah proof
Zahir al-Riwayah analogizes to a mortgagor building on mortgaged property: because the pre-emption right is a confirmed right (haqq muta'akkid) attached from the moment of sale, the buyer's actions are annulled. This explains why removal is possible even for waqf or mosque.
The buyer built without the preemptor's permission, unlike the donee or fasid buyer.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its implications
The text explicitly sides with Zahir al-Riwayah (وجه ظاهر الرواية). Thus, the operative ruling is that the preemptor may force removal or take with compensation. An exception is made for cultivation (istihsan) and decoration (no independent value).
The thamarah al-khilaf: if preemptor cannot force removal, he must either pay for the building or lose his right entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة باب طلب الشفعة
بنى المشتري أو غرس أو كلف قلعهما وإن قلعهما الشفيع فاستحقت بالثمن فقط رجع كلف قلعهما) يعني إذا بنى المشتري أو غرس في الأرض المشفوعة ثم قضى للشفيع بالشفعة فالشفيع بالخيار إن شاء أخذها بالثمن وقيمة البناء والغرس مقلوعاً، وإن شاء كلف المشتري قلعه فيأخذ الأرض فارغة. وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يكلف بالقلع ولكنه بالخيار إن شاء أخذها بالثمن وقيمة البناء والغرس وإن شاء ترك وبه قال الإمام الشافعي ومالك لأنه ليس متعدياً في البناء والغرس لثبوت ملكه فيه بالشراء فلا يعامل بأحكام العدوان فصار كالموهوب له والمشتري شراء فاسداً عند الإمام، وكما إذا زرعها المشتري فإن كل واحد منهم لا يكلف بالقلع لتصرفه في ملكه وهذا لأن ضرر الشفيع بالزام قيمة البناء والغرس أهون من ضرر المشتري بالقلع لأن الشفيع يحصل له بمقابلة الثمن عوضان وهو البناء والغرس فلا يعد ضرراً، ولم يحصل للمشتري بمقابلة القلع شيء فكان الأول أهون فكان أولى بالتحمل. ووجه ظاهر الرواية أنه بنى في محل تعلق به حق متأكد لغيره من غير تسليط منه فينتقض كالراهن إذا بنى في المرهون ولهذا تنتقض جميع تصرفات المشتري حتى الوقف والمسجد والمقبرة بخلاف الموهوب على قول أبي حنيفة والمشتري شراء فاسداً لأنه فعل بتسليط من المالك ولهذا لا ينتقض تصرفهما. وفي الزرع القياس أن يقلع إلا أننا استحسناً ولذا قلنا لا يقلع لأن له نهاية، وليس على الشفيع كبير ضرر بالتأخير لأنه يترك بأجرته. فإن قلت: الاسترداد عندهما بعد البناء فإن جواز الاسترداد دينا في أنه لا يكلف القلع بل يقتضي القلع كما في الشفيع قلت: يجوز أن يكون مراده بقوله والمشتري شراء فساداً احتجاج من أبي يوسف عن أبي حنيفة بمذهب أبي حنيفة كما أفصح به صاحب غاية البيان وهذا بعيد والأوجه أن يقال : إن لأبي يوسف في البناء بعد الشراء الفاسد القول المذكور والثاني كما قال الإمام ذكره في الإيضاح . قيد بما ذكر احترازاً عن الزخرفة وفي قاضيخان ولو اشترى الرجل داراً وزخرفها بالنقوش شيء كثير كان للشفيع الخيار إن شاء أخذها وأعطاه ما زاد فيها وإن شاء ترك اهـ. قال في المحيط : لأن نقص صفته لا يمكن وفيه نظر لأن المشتري إذا بنى على الدار المشفوعة كان أن ينقض البناء ويأخذ الدار ويعطيه ما زاد فيها وأجيب بأن البناء إذا قلع له قيمة في الجملة بخلاف الزخرفة. قوله أو بنى أو غرس مثال وليس بقيد لما في المحيط : ولو أن المشتري زرعها رطبة أو كرما يؤمر بقلعه كالبناء ..
Book of Shuf'a, Chapter of Seeking Shuf'a. If the buyer built or planted, and he forced their removal, and if the preemptor removed them and then the property was reclaimed, he recovers only the price. 'Forced their removal' meaning: if the buyer built or planted in the preempted land and then judgment was given for the preemptor, the preemptor has the choice: if he wishes, he may take it for the price and the value of the building and planting as removed, and if he wishes, he may force the buyer to remove them and take the land empty. From Abu Yusuf that he is not forced to remove, but he has the choice: if he wishes, take it for the price and the value of the building and planting, and if he wishes, leave. Imam al-Shafi'i and Malik also said this, because the buyer is not an aggressor in building and planting due to his ownership being established by purchase, so he is not treated with the rules of transgression. Thus he is like a donee and a buyer in a fasid purchase according to the Imam, and like if the buyer planted it. Each of them is not forced to remove because he acted in his own property. This is because the harm to the preemptor by imposing the value of building and planting is lighter than the harm to the buyer by removal, because the preemptor receives two substitutes in exchange for the price—the building and planting—so it is not considered harm, while the buyer receives nothing in exchange for removal, so the first is lighter and more deserving of bearing. The face of Zahir al-Riwayah is that he built in a place to which a confirmed right of another is attached without his permission, so it is annulled, like a mortgagor who builds on the mortgaged property. For this reason, all transactions of the buyer are annulled, even a waqf, mosque, and graveyard. Unlike the donee, according to Abu Hanifa's statement, and the buyer in a fasid purchase, because he acted with the permission of the owner, thus their transactions are not annulled. Regarding cultivation, the analogy is that it should be removed, except that we applied istihsan, therefore we say it is not removed because it has an end (season), and there is no great harm to the preemptor by delay because he leaves it for its rent. If you say: the recovery from them after building, then the permissibility of recovery as a debt is that he is not forced to remove, but rather it necessitates removal as with the preemptor? I say: it is possible that his meaning by his statement 'and the buyer in a fasid purchase' is a proof from Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa according to Abu Hanifa's school, as the author of Ghayat al-Bayan clarified, and this is far-fetched. The more appropriate is to say: that Abu Yusuf regarding building after a fasid purchase has the mentioned statement, and the second as the Imam said, mentioned in al-Idah. He restricted by what was mentioned as a precaution against decoration. In Qadikhan: if a man bought a house and decorated it with many paintings, the preemptor has the choice: if he wishes, he takes it and gives him the increase in it, and if he wishes, he leaves. End. He said in al-Muhit: because the deficiency of its description is not possible, and there is consideration because if the buyer built on the preempted house, he would demolish the building, take the house, and give him the increase in it. It was answered that the building, when removed, has some value, unlike decoration. His statement 'or built or planted' is an example and not a restriction, as in al-Muhit: and if the buyer planted it with fresh plants or grapevines, he is ordered to remove them like the building.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the preemptor's rights when the buyer has built or planted on the property. The Zahir al-Riwayah position (Imam Abu Hanifa) gives the preemptor an option: either take the property with compensation for the value of the building/planting as if removed, or force the buyer to remove them and take the land empty. Abu Yusuf, along with Shafi'i and Malik, holds that the buyer cannot be forced to remove the building; the preemptor may only choose to take with compensation or leave the property. The passage provides detailed reasoning: Abu Yusuf argues that the buyer is not a transgressor because he owned the property, so he is like a donee or a buyer in a fasid purchase who are not forced to remove. The Zahir al-Riwayah responds by analogy to a mortgagor building on mortgaged property: since the buyer built in a place where another's right is attached, his actions are subject to annulment. An exception is made for cultivation, where istihsan (juristic preference) dictates that the crop is not removed because it has a seasonal end and the preemptor can take rent, but the analogy would require removal. The text also addresses decoration (zakhrafa), where the preemptor does not have the option to force removal because decoration has no independent value after removal, unlike building. The example of building and planting is not exhaustive; the same ruling applies to other improvements like vineyards or fresh plants.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption right of a co-owner or neighbor to buy property sold to an outsider.
قلع (qal'): Removal of a building or planting from the land by demolishing or uprooting.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted position from Imam Muhammad's six books (the authoritative Hanafi position).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah, what options does the preemptor have when the buyer has built on the property?
A) He may only take the building by paying its full market value.
B) He may either take the building by paying its value as demolished or force the buyer to remove it.
C) He must force the buyer to remove the building at the buyer's expense.
D) He cannot force removal but may take the building by paying its value.

Key Takeaway
The preemptor's confirmed right (haqq muta'akkid) allows him to force removal of unauthorised constructions made by the buyer.

Preemptor's Building and Istihqaq: The Role of Deception
Intra-Hanafi dispute on preemptor's compensation after istihqaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a preemptor does not recover the value of improvements after istihqaq and distinguish this from the buyer's position.

Core Idea
Deception determines recourse: preemptor under compulsion bears his own loss.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on the preemptor's right to recover the value of improvements when the property is reclaimed by a true owner. The key distinction hinges on the concept of ghurur (deception). Understanding this distinction clarifies the Hanafi principle that liability follows authority and inducement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The preemptor built or planted on the land after taking it by shuf'ah, then a third party established ownership (istihqaq) and forced the preemptor to remove the structures. The dispute is about what the preemptor can recover from the seller or the buyer.
2
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Abu Hanifa holds that the preemptor recovers only the price he paid, not the value of the building or planting.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
State Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf argues that the preemptor recovers both the price and the value of the improvements, because he is like a deceived buyer.
Abu Yusuf analogizes to the buyer (mubtā'i).
4
Analyze the distinguishing factor
The buyer is deceived (maghrur) by the seller and acts under his authority. The preemptor, however, took the land compulsorily (jabran) with no deception or authority from the seller or buyer. Therefore, the preemptor bears the loss of his own improvements.
The concept of ghurur is central.
5
Apply the analogy of the captive slave-woman
If the original owner recovers a captive slave-woman from a new owner after she has borne a child, the new owner does not recover the value of the child because there was no deception. Similarly, the preemptor has no claim.
This reinforces the principle that without ghurur, no compensation beyond the price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للشفيع قال رحمه الله : ( وإن قلعهما الشفيع فاستحقت رجع بالثمن فقط) يعني أن الشفيع إذا أخذ الأرض بالشفعة فبنى أو غرس ثم استحقت فكلف المستحق الشفيع بالقلع فقلع البناء والغرس رجع الشفيع على المشتري إن أخذها منه أو على البائع إن أخذها منه بالثمن ولا يرجع بقيمة البناء والغرس وعن أبي يوسف أنه يرجع بذلك كالمشتري. والفرق بينه وبين المشتري أن المشتري مغرور ومن جهة البائع ومسلط عليه من جهته ولا غرور ولا تسليط للشفيع من جهة المشتري ولا البائع لأن الشفيع أخذها منه جبراً، ونظيره الجارية المأسورة إذا استردها المالك القديم من مالكها الجديد بقيمتها أو بالثمن فاستولدها ثم استحقت وضمن قيمة الولد رجع عليه بما دفع له من القيمة أو الثمن ولا يرجع بقيمة الولد لأنه لم يغره بخلاف ما لو كان مشترياً حيث يرجع بهما على البائع لأنه مغرور من جهته.
For the preemptor: He (the author) said: 'And if the preemptor removes them and then the property is reclaimed, he recovers only the price.' Meaning: if the preemptor took the land by pre-emption and then built or planted, and then the property was reclaimed, and the claimant forced the preemptor to remove, so he removed the building and planting, the preemptor recovers from the buyer if he took it from him, or from the seller if he took it from him, the price, and he does not recover the value of the building and planting. From Abu Yusuf that he recovers that as well like the buyer. The difference between him and the buyer is that the buyer is deceived (maghrur) and from the side of the seller and was given authority from him, while there is no deception nor authority for the preemptor from the side of the buyer or the seller, because the preemptor took it from him compulsorily. Its analogy is the captive slave-woman: if the original owner recovers her from her new owner by her value or price, and then she gave birth and then she is reclaimed, and he guarantees the value of the child, he recovers from him what he paid of value or price, and does not recover the value of the child because he did not deceive him. Unlike if he was a buyer, where he recovers both from the seller because he is deceived from his side.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where the preemptor himself has built or planted on the property after taking possession via pre-emption, and then the property is reclaimed by a third party (istihqaq). The ruling is that the preemptor can only recover the price he paid (either from the buyer or seller), not the value of the building or planting. Abu Yusuf disagrees, analogizing to a buyer who would recover both the price and the value of improvements because he was deceived. The text explains the distinction: the buyer is deceived (maghrur) by the seller and acts with his permission, so the seller is liable for all losses. The preemptor, however, took the property forcibly (jabran), without any deception or authority from the seller or buyer, so he bears the loss of his own improvements. The analogy of a captive slave-woman reinforces this: if the original owner recovers her after she had a child, he does not compensate the new owner for the child because there was no deception.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): The establishment of ownership by a third party, often through proof of prior right, voiding the preemptor's possession.
غرور (ghurur): Deception or misrepresentation that induces a contract. The deceived party (maghrur) has recourse for losses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the preemptor not recover the value of his building when the property is reclaimed, according to the Hanafi position?
A) Because the preemptor was negligent in building.
B) Because the preemptor was not deceived and did not act under anyone's authority.
C) Because the building belonged to the original owner.
D) Because the preemptor could have avoided building.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the preemptor who builds on the property he took by force bears the loss of improvements if the property is reclaimed, unlike a buyer who is deceived.

Price Reduction for Deterioration and Fruit Inclusion in Pre-emption
Rulings on how price is adjusted for damaged improvements and inclusion of fruits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a preemptor pays the full price or a reduced price when buildings collapse or trees dry up, and identify when fruit is included in the pre-emption.

Core Idea
Natural deterioration does not reduce the pre-emption price; fruit is included only by stipulation or subsequent growth (istihsan).

When a preemptor exercises his right of shufʿa, the price is normally the full amount paid by the buyer. However, complications arise if buildings have collapsed or trees have been cut. The Hanafi school addresses these scenarios with detailed rules. In this lesson, we examine the key rulings from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq on price adjustments for damaged improvements and the inclusion of fruit.

Guided Path
1
Classify the cause of deterioration
Determine whether the building collapsed or trees dried up naturally, or whether the buyer intentionally demolished them. Natural causes do not lead to price reduction unless the buyer retains material remnants.
Natural collapse: no deduction. Buyer's demolition: price is apportioned.
2
Check for remaining material
If any demolition material (naqd) or wood remains and the buyer takes it, the price is reduced proportionally. If nothing remains, the preemptor pays the full price.
Remaining material counts as existing wealth belonging to the buyer.
3
Apply the rule for buyer's demolition
If the buyer demolishes the building, the preemptor takes only the land for its share of the price, because the building was intentionally destroyed. Demolition by a third party is treated the same.
The price is divided based on the value of land and building at the time of contract.
4
Determine ownership of demolition material
Once the building is demolished, the material (bricks, timber) belongs to the buyer, not the preemptor, because it is no longer an appurtenance to the land.
The preemptor only takes the land and what is attached; separate items are not included.
5
Evaluate inclusion of fruit
Fruit is included in the pre-emption only if it was explicitly stipulated in the sale or if it grew after the buyer took possession. This is based on istiḥsān because of physical connection, analogizing to a sold animal giving birth before delivery.
The pure qiyas would exclude fruit; istiḥsān overrides due to attachment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبكل الثمن أن خربت الدار وجف الشجر) يعني لو اشترى أرضاً فيها بناء أو غرس فانهدم البناء من غير صنع أحد يأخذها الشفيع بكل الثمن ولا يسقط من الثمن شيء لأنهما تابعان للأرض يدخلان في بيعها من غير ذكر فلا يقابلها شيء من الثمن ولهذا يبيعها في هذه الحالة مرابحة من غير بيان بخلاف ما إذا تلف بعض الأرض بغرق حيث يسقط من الثمن بحصته لأن الغالب بعض الأصل هذا إذا انهدم البناء ولم يبق له نقض ولا من الشجر شيء من حطب أو خشب، وأما إذا بقي شيء من ذلك وأخذه المشتري فلا بد من سقوط بعض الثمن بحصته ذلك لأنه عين مال قائم بقي يحبس عند المشتري فيكون له حصة من الثمن فيقسم الثمن على قيمة الدار يوم العقد وعلى قيمة النقض يوم الأخذ. قيد بقوله «حق الشجر» ليخرج الثمر إذا هلك من غير صنع . قال في التتارخانية : ولو هلك الثمر من غير صنع أحد ولم يبق منه شيء سقط حصته من الثمن بخلاف البناء وسيأتي ما يخالفه. قال رحمه الله : (وبحصة العرصة أن نقض المشتري النياء يعني يأخذ الشفيع العرصة بحصتها من الثمن إن نقض المشتري البناء لأنه صار مقصوداً بالإتلاف ويقابله شيء من الثمن فيقسم الثمن على قيمة الأرض والبناء . يوم العقد، ونقض الأجنبي النياء كنقض المشتري. وفي التتارخانية : لو لم يهدم المشتري البناء ولكن باعه من غيره من غير إرضاء ثم حضر الشفيع فله أن ينقض البيع ويأخذ الكل وكذا النبات والنخل . قال رحمه الله : (والنقض له) يعني النقض للمشتري لأن الشفيع للمشتري لأن الشفيع إنما كان يأخذه بطريق التبعية للعرصة وقد زالت بالانفصال. رحمه الله : ( وبثمرها إن ابتاع أرضاً ونخلاً وثمراً أو أثمر في يده) يعني يأخذها الشفيع مع ثمرها إن كان المشتري اشترى الأرض مع الثمر بأن شرطه في البيع أو أثمر عند المشتري بعد راء لأن الثمر لا يدخل في البيع إلا بالشرط بخلاف النخل والقياس أن لا يكون له أخذ الثمر لعدم التبعية كالمتاع الموضوع فيها. وجه الاستحسان أن الاتصال خلقة صار تبعاً من وجه ولا يتولد من البيع فيسري إليه الحق الثابت في الأصل كالمبيعة إذا ولدت قبل القبض فإن المشتري يملك الولد تبعاً للأم كذا هنا. وفي الخانية : لو اشترى قرية فيها أشجار ونخل فقطع المشتري بعض الأشجار وهدم بعض البناء فحضر الشفيع يأخذ الأرض وما لم يقطع من الأشجار وما لم يهدم من البناء وليس له أن يأخذها بالشفعة ويقسم الثمن على قيمة البناء
And with the entire price if the house falls into ruin and the trees dry up. That is, if he bought land containing a building or planting, and the building collapses without anyone's action, the preemptor takes it for the entire price and nothing is deducted from the price, because they are appurtenances to the land, included in its sale without mention, so nothing of the price corresponds to them. For this reason, one sells it in this condition as murabaha without disclosure, unlike if part of the land itself is lost by flooding, where the price is reduced proportionally because it is part of the original. This is if the building collapsed and no material (naqd) remains, and no part of the tree such as firewood or timber. But if something of that remains and the buyer takes it, then part of the price must be deducted proportionally, because it is existing wealth that remains held by the buyer, so it has a share of the price; thus the price is divided on the value of the house on the day of contract and on the value of the material on the day of taking. He restricted by his statement 'right of the tree' to exclude fruit if it perishes without human action. He said in al-Tatarakhaniyya: if fruit perishes without anyone's action and nothing remains, its share of the price is dropped, unlike the building, and something contradicting this will come. He (the author) said: And with the share of the plot if the buyer demolishes the building. That is, the preemptor takes the plot for its share of the price if the buyer demolishes the building, because it became intended for destruction, and something of the price corresponds to it; thus the price is divided on the value of land and building on the day of contract. Demolition by a third party is like demolition by the buyer. In al-Tatarakhaniyya: if the buyer did not demolish the building but sold it to someone else without permission, then the preemptor arrives, he has the right to rescind the sale and take the whole, and likewise plants and palm trees. He said: And the demolition material is for him (the buyer). That is, the demolition material belongs to the buyer, because the preemptor only takes it as an appurtenance to the plot, and it has ceased by separation. He said: And with its fruit if he bought land and palm trees and fruit, or if it bore fruit in his hand. That is, the preemptor takes it with its fruit if the buyer bought the land with the fruit by stipulating it in the sale, or if it bore fruit with the buyer after purchase, because fruit is not included in the sale except by stipulation, unlike palm trees. The analogy is that he should not take the fruit because it lacks appurtenance, like goods placed therein. The face of istihsan is that physical connection makes it an appurtenance in some respect, and it is not generated from the sale, so the right established in the origin extends to it, like a sold animal if it gives birth before delivery, where the buyer owns the offspring appurtenant to the mother. In al-Khaniyya: if he bought a village containing trees and palm trees, and the buyer cut some trees and demolished some buildings, then the preemptor arrives, he takes the land and what was not cut from the trees and what was not demolished from the buildings, and he does not take it by pre-emption and divide the price on the value of the building.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage contains several rulings on how the purchase price is treated when improvements deteriorate or are removed. First, if the building collapses or trees dry up naturally, the preemptor pays the full price because these are appurtenances to the land and no portion of the price is allocated to them. However, if the buyer retains any remaining material (like bricks or wood), then the price is reduced proportionally because the buyer keeps that value. Second, if the buyer demolishes the building himself, the preemptor takes only the land for its share of the price, since the building was intentionally destroyed and its value corresponds to part of the price. Demolition by a third party is treated the same. Third, if the buyer sells the building separately, the preemptor can rescind that sale and take everything. Fourth, the demolition material (e.g., bricks) belongs to the buyer, not the preemptor, because the preemptor only takes the land and appurtenances; once the building is separated, it is no longer appurtenant. Fifth, regarding fruit: if the buyer bought land with trees and the fruit was explicitly stipulated in the sale, or if the fruit grew after purchase, the preemptor takes the fruit along with the land. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) because the fruit is physically connected to the trees, similar to an unborn animal that goes to the buyer. The analogy (qiyas) would suggest otherwise, but istihsan prevails. However, if the buyer cuts down trees or demolishes buildings before the preemptor arrives, the preemptor takes only what remains and the price is apportioned accordingly.

Key Terms
عَرصة (arsa): The plot of land itself, as distinct from buildings or trees.
نَقض (naqd): Demolition material (e.g., bricks, timber) removed from a building.
ثمر (thamar): Fruit on trees that is already existing or grows after purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a building on preempted land collapses naturally (no human action) and no material remains, how much does the preemptor pay?
A) He pays the full price.
B) He pays the price minus the value of the building.
C) He pays half the price.
D) He pays nothing.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi pre-emption, natural deterioration of improvements does not reduce the price unless the buyer retains material, and fruit is included only if stipulated or grown post-purchase (istihsan).

Pre-emption Price When Buyer Cuts Fruit After Contract
Ruling on price reduction when pre-emptor takes after buyer cuts fruit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on price reduction when a buyer cuts fruit that appeared after the contract, and evaluate Abu Yusuf's initial analogy and his retraction.

Core Idea
Fruit appearing after contract is not part of the sale, so the pre-emptor pays full price; Abu Yusuf initially disagreed but retracted.

Pre-emption (shuf'ah) gives a co-owner the right to buy a sold share, but what happens when the buyer cuts fruit from the property before the pre-emptor takes it? The answer depends on whether the fruit existed at the time of the contract. This lesson examines the Hanafi position and an intra-madhhab dispute where Abu Yusuf initially proposed a different ruling.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the Two Scenarios
Identify the two cases: (1) fruit was present at the contract and included in the sale; (2) fruit appeared after the contract. Note that the rule differs in each.
The first scenario leads to price reduction; the second does not.
2
Identify the Ruling for Second Scenario
State the final Hanafi position: when fruit appears after the contract and is cut by the buyer, the pre-emptor takes the land and trees at the full price, with no reduction.
The fruit was never part of the sale, so its loss does not affect the price.
3
Trace Abu Yusuf's Initial Position
Understand Abu Yusuf's initial opinion: he analogized the pre-emptor's situation to a seller consuming fruit after delivery, arguing for a reduction in price.
He saw the buyer as akin to a seller before delivery, with the pre-emptor as the buyer.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response and Retraction
The final opinion rejected the analogy because the pre-emptor takes from the buyer, not from the seller, and the fruit occurred in the buyer's ownership. Abu Yusuf retracted, and this is the relied-upon view (zahir al-riwaya).
The key distinction: the fruit belonged to the buyer, so no reduction is due.
5
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: under the initial opinion, the pre-emptor would pay less; under the final opinion, the pre-emptor pays the full price.
This difference affects the actual amount the pre-emptor must pay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يجب فيه الشفعة وما لا يجب
وإن جذه المشتري سقط حصته من الثمن .
إنما تجب الشفعة في عقار ملك بعوض هو مال لا في عرض وفلك وبناء ونخل بيعا
والأرض، فما أصاب البناء سقط، وما أصاب العرصة يأخذها به وينقض بناء المشتري الذي أحدثه وهذا القول ظاهر الرواية. قال رحمه الله : (وإن جذه المشتري سقط حصته من الثمن يعني في الفصل الأول وهو ما اشتراها بثمرها بالشرط فكان له فيسقط من الثمن بحصته، وإن هلك بآفة سماوية فكذلك لأنه لما دخل في البيع صار أصلاً فسقط حصته من الثمن بفواته . وأما في الأصل الثاني فيأخذ الأرض والنخل بجميع الثمن لأن الثمن لم يكن موجوداً عند العقد فلا يقابله شيء من الثمن، وكان أبو يوسف يقول أولاً إنه يحط من الثمن في الفصل الثاني لأن حال المشتري مع الشفيع كحال البائع مع المشتري قبل القبض. ولو أكل البائع الثمر الحادث بعد القبض سقط حصته من الثمن فكذا هنا ثم رجع إلى ما ذكر في أنه لا يسقط شيء من الثمن لأن الشفيع يأخذ بما قام على المشتري وهو قائم عليه المبيع بدون الثمن بجميع الثمن بخلاف ما إذا كانت موجودة عند العقد لأنه دخل في البيع قصداً، وبخلاف الحادث عند البائع قبل القبض لأنه حدث على ملك المشتري فيكون له حصة من الثمن بالاستهلاك، وليس للشفيع أن يأخذ الثمن بعد الجذاذ في الفصلين لزوال التبعية بالانفصال قبل الأخذ والله تعالى أعلم .
Chapter on what pre-emption applies to and what does not. If the buyer cuts it (the fruit), his share of the price falls. Pre-emption is only obligatory in real estate owned by a consideration that is wealth, not in movable goods, a ship, a building, and palm trees sold without land. So whatever pertains to the building falls, and whatever pertains to the land he takes with it and demolishes the buyer's newly constructed building—this is the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya). He (the author) said: ‘If the buyer cuts it, his share of the price falls’—meaning in the first case, which is when he bought it with its fruit by stipulation, so he owned it; then the share of the price falls, and if it perishes by a heavenly calamity, likewise, because when it entered the sale it became an asset, so its share of the price falls due to its loss. As for the second case, he takes the land and the palm trees with the entire price because the fruit—if it is new—was not present at the contract, so nothing of the price corresponds to it. Abu Yusuf initially said that the price is reduced in the second case because the situation of the buyer with the pre-emptor is like that of the seller with the buyer before delivery. If the seller consumed the fruit that appeared after delivery, his share of the price falls; so too here. Then he retracted to what was mentioned, that nothing of the price falls because the pre-emptor takes what stands with the buyer, and the property stands with him without the fruit for the entire price, contrary to when it was present at the contract because it entered the sale intentionally, and contrary to the fruit that appeared with the seller before delivery because it occurred in the buyer's ownership, so he has a share of the price due to consumption. The pre-emptor may not take the fruit after cutting in both cases due to the removal of dependency by separation before taking. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the pre-emptor's right when the buyer has cut fruit from the property. The matn states that if the buyer cuts the fruit, his share of the price is deducted from the total sale price. The commentary explains two scenarios: (1) when fruit is present at the time of contract and included in the sale, and (2) when fruit appears after the contract but before pre-emption. In the first scenario, the fruit is part of the sale; if cut, its portion of the price is deducted, and the pre-emptor takes the rest at the reduced price. In the second scenario, the fruit is not part of the sale, so the pre-emptor takes the land and trees at the full price. Abu Yusuf initially analogized to a seller consuming fruit after delivery, arguing for reduction, but he retracted, adopting the view that no reduction occurs because the pre-emptor takes the property as it stands. The final ruling (mufta bihi) is that the price is not reduced in the second scenario, and the pre-emptor cannot take the fruit after it has been cut since it is no longer attached.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): Right of pre-emption to purchase jointly owned property from a co-owner or adjacent property.
جذاذ (jadhādh): The act of cutting fruit from the tree.
ثمن (thaman): Price paid for the property; may be reduced if part of the sale is lost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second scenario (fruit appears after the contract), what is the Hanafi ruling on the pre-emption price when the buyer cuts the fruit?
A) The price is reduced by the value of the fruit.
B) The pre-emptor pays the full price.
C) The pre-emptor cannot take the property.
D) The price is doubled.

Key Takeaway
Fruit that appears after the contract is not part of the sale, so its cutting does not reduce the pre-emption price; Abu Yusuf's initial analogy was retracted.

Definition and Conditions for Pre-emption
Detailed explanation of what pre-emption applies to and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for shuf'a and explain why the Hanafi school includes indivisible properties while the Shafi'i school excludes them.

Core Idea
Pre-emption applies to all real estate acquired by a wealth consideration; its purpose is to prevent harm from bad company, not just partition costs.

Pre-emption (shuf'a) is a right to step into a sale of adjacent property, but not every property qualifies. The Hanafi school sets clear conditions: the property must be real estate, acquired by a valid exchange of wealth. This lesson unpacks these conditions, then dives into a key disagreement with the Shafi'i school: do indivisible properties like wells and lanes trigger pre-emption? The answer depends on the underlying rationale of shuf'a.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and conditions
The matn states: 'Shuf'a applies only to real estate owned by a consideration that is wealth.' Break down each term: عقار (real estate), بعوض (by a consideration), هو مال (that is wealth).
This sets the boundaries of shuf'a.
2
Locate the inter-madhhab dispute
The author notes that عقار includes divisible and indivisible properties, then presents the Shafi'i objection that indivisible properties (wells, mills, bathhouses, rivers, roads) are excluded. Identify who disagrees and their evidence.
The dispute is based on different purposes of shuf'a.
3
Contrast the underlying principles
Al-Shafi'i holds that shuf'a exists to avoid the cost of partition (harm of dividing). The Hanafis hold it is to avoid harm from bad company (bad mixing). Which principle leads to which conclusion?
Hanafi: harm can occur even in indivisible shared properties.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence of the dispute. In a shared well or lane, Hanafis allow pre-emption; Shafi'is do not. How does this affect a neighbor's right?
The difference is real and affects property transactions.
5
Apply to examples from the text
Work through the examples given: a thicket with reeds and fish, land with growing crops, waqf properties. Determine whether shuf'a applies in each based on the conditions.
Apply the rule, not just memorize examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر تفصيل ما تجب فيه الشفعة وما لا تجب بعد ذكر نفس الوجوب مجملاً لأن التفصيل بعد الإجمال أوقع في النفس ؛ كذا في العناية قال رحمه الله : (إنما تجب الشفعة في عقار ملك بعوض هو مال) قوله «في عقار يتناول ما يقسم وما لا يقسم. وقال الشافعي : لا تجب فيما لا يقسم كالبئر والرحا والحمام والنهر والطريق وهذا مبني على أصل عنده وهو أن الشفعة تجب لدفع ضرر أجرة القسام عنده وعندنا لدفع ضرر سوء العشرة. واحترز بقوله بعوض عما إذا ملك بالهبة فإن الشفعة لا تجب فيها ، وقوله هو مال عما إذا ملك بعوض غير مال كالمهر والخلع والصلح عن دم عمد والعتق فإن الشفعة لا تجب في هذه الأشياء على ما بينه قريباً، والعقار لغة الضيعة، وقيل ماله أصل من دار وضيعة نقله الإمام الطرزي. ونقل الشراح هنا العقار كل ماله أصل من دار وضيعة .اهـ. فهو مطابق للتفسير الثاني. ونقل الجوهري في فصل العين من باب الراء : العقار بالفتح الأرض والضياع والنخل ومنه قولهم ماله دار ولا عقار والجمع ضياع. وفي فصل الضاد من باب العين: الضيعة بلا عرصة ودار جعلت مهراً أو أجرة أو بدل خلق أو بدل صلح عن دم عمد أو عوض عتق أو وهبت بلا عوض مشروط وإن بيعت بخيار البائع أو بيعت فاسدا ما لم العقار اهـ. وفي كلامه اختلال لأنه فسر العقار أولاً بما يشمل الأقسام الثلاثة الأرض والضياع والنخل، ثم فسر الضيعة بالعقار فلزم تفسير الأخص بالأعم كما ترى. وفي المحيط : ويدخل في الحمام ما كان مركباً في بنيانه دون المنفصل كالقصعة، ويدخل في الرحا الحجر الأسفل دون الأعلى لأنه مبني في الأرض، ولو اشترى أجمة فيها قصب وسمك يوجد بلا صيد استحق الأجمة والقصب بالشفعة دون السمك لأنه منقول والقصب يشعب في الأرض. وفي التتارخانية وإنما تجب في الأراضي التي تملك رقابها حتى لا تجب في الأراضي التي جازها الإمام للمسلمين يدفعها بزراعة، وإنما تجب لحق الملك في الأراضي لو بيعت دار بجنبها دار الوقف فلا شفعة للواقف ولا للمتولي لعدم الملك؛ كذا في المحيط وغيره. وفي السراجية رجل له دار في أرض الوقف فلا شفعة له، ولو باع عمارته فلا شفعة لجاره. وفي التجريد ولو جعل داره مسجداً وأفرزه وجعل بابه إلى الطريق فبیعت دار إلى جنب المسجد لم يكن للواقف ولا للمتولي شفعة لعدم الملك. وفي المحيط وغيره : ما لا يجوز بيعه في العقارات كالأوقاف والحانوت المسبل فلا شفعة في ذلك عند من يرى جواز الوقف. وفي المبسوط : لو اشترى أرضاً فيها شجر صغار فأثمرت أو فيها زرع فأدرك فللشفيع أن يأخذ ذلك بجميع الثمر لاتصاله بالأرض اهـ.
Mention of the detail of what pre-emption applies to and what does not apply after mentioning the obligation itself in summary, because detail after summary is more impactful; likewise in al-'Inayah. He (the author) said: 'Pre-emption applies only to real estate owned by a consideration that is wealth.' His statement 'in real estate' includes what is divisible and what is not. Al-Shafi'i said: It does not apply to what is not divisible, like a well, a mill, a bathhouse, a river, and a road. This is based on his principle that pre-emption is established to avoid the harm of the cost of partition, whereas with us it is to avoid the harm of bad company. He excluded by his statement 'by a consideration' from when it is owned by gift, for pre-emption does not apply there; and his statement 'that is wealth' from when it is owned by a consideration that is not wealth, like mahr, khul', compromise for intentional homicide, and manumission, for pre-emption does not apply in these matters, as he will clarify soon. Real estate linguistically is al-diy'ah (landed property), and it is said: everything that has a foundation from a house and a landed property, as transmitted by Imam al-Tarazi. The commentators here transmitted that real estate is everything that has a foundation from a house and landed property. End quote. So it corresponds to the second interpretation. Al-Jawhari transmitted: 'al-aqar with fatha is land, estates, and palm trees; from it is their saying: he has neither a house nor real estate; plural is diya'.' And in the chapter of dad from the letter 'ayn: 'al-diy'ah is without a courtyard, and a house made as mahr or rent or substitute for creation [i.e., compensation for injury] or substitute for compromise for intentional homicide or compensation for manumission or given as gift without a condition, even if sold with option of the seller or sold invalidly, as long as the real estate...' End quote. In his speech there is confusion because he first interpreted real estate to include three categories: land, estates, and palm trees; then he interpreted al-diy'ah as real estate, necessitating interpretation of the more specific by the more general, as you see. And in al-Muhit: The bathhouse includes what is fixed in its building, not what is separate like a bowl; and the mill includes the lower stone, not the upper, because it is built into the ground. If one buys a thicket containing reeds and fish found without hunting, the thicket and reeds are subject to pre-emption, not the fish, because it is movable, and the reeds are rooted in the ground. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: Pre-emption applies only to lands whose titles are owned, so it does not apply to lands that the Imam has given permission to Muslims to cultivate. It applies due to the right of ownership in lands; if a house adjacent to a waqf house is sold, there is no pre-emption for the waqf founder or the trustee because they lack ownership; likewise in al-Muhit and others. In al-Sirajiyya: A man has a house in waqf land, there is no pre-emption for him; if he sells his structure, there is no pre-emption for his neighbor. In al-Tajrid: If one makes his house a mosque and separates it and makes its door to the road, then a house adjacent to the mosque is sold, the waqf founder and trustee have no pre-emption due to lack of ownership. In al-Muhit and others: What cannot be sold among real estates, like waqfs and endowed shops, there is no pre-emption in that according to those who deem waqf permissible. In al-Mabsut: If one buys land containing small trees that bear fruit or crops that ripen, the pre-emptor may take all that with the fruit because of its connection to the land. End quote.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the definition and conditions for pre-emption (shuf'a). The matn states the general rule: shuf'a applies only to real estate (عقار) acquired by a consideration that is wealth (عوض هو مال). The commentary clarifies each term. It begins by noting that the detail after summary is more impactful. The term 'عقار' includes both divisible and indivisible properties. Then an inter-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i is presented: al-Shafi'i excludes indivisible properties like wells, mills, bathhouses, rivers, and roads because his principle is that shuf'a is to avoid the harm of the cost of partition; whereas the Hanafi principle is to avoid the harm of bad company. The condition 'بعوض' excludes gifts, and 'هو مال' excludes considerations that are not wealth, such as mahr, khul', diyat, etc. The passage then discusses linguistic definitions of 'عقار' and 'ضيعة', noting confusion in a citation. It gives examples from various fiqh works: what is included in a bathhouse, mill; the case of reeds and fish in a thicket; the condition that the land must be owned (milk al-raqaba), excluding lands held by permission (iqta'); and the lack of shuf'a for waqf properties because there is no owner. Finally, it mentions that if land with trees or crops is bought, the pre-emptor takes all including the fruit because of attachment.

Key Terms
عقار (aqār): Real estate: land, buildings, and palm trees that are rooted; everything that has a foundation.
عوض (iwaḍ): Consideration given in exchange; shuf'a requires a valid exchange.
مال (māl): Wealth: something of value that people customarily possess and trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school include indivisible properties like wells and bathhouses in pre-emption while al-Shafi'i excludes them?
A) Hanafis rely on a different hadith.
B) Hanafis view the purpose of shuf'a as avoiding bad company, not partition costs.
C) Hanafis consider wells as similar to houses.
D) Al-Shafi'i does not recognize pre-emption in any real estate.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a applies only to owned real estate acquired by a wealth consideration; the Hanafi school includes indivisible properties because the purpose is to prevent harm from bad company.

Cases Where Pre-Emption Does Not Apply
Ruling on movable goods, ships, buildings without land, and non-wealth considerations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for when shuf'a does not apply and explain the dispute with Imam Malik regarding ships.

Core Idea
Pre-emption is limited to real estate acquired through wealth-based exchange; it does not extend to movables, ships, or non-wealth considerations.

Pre-emption (shuf'a) is a right to replace a buyer in a sale, but this right is not unlimited. The Hanafi school carefully delineates its boundaries: it applies only to real estate acquired through a valid exchange of wealth. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains several key exclusions, including a well-known disagreement with Imam Malik over whether ships are subject to shuf'a.

Guided Path
1
Identify all items excluded from shuf'a
Read the source passage and list the specific items mentioned: movable goods, ships, buildings sold without land, palm trees without land, and houses acquired through mahr, rent, compensation, or gifts without condition.
These are all cases where the Hanafi school denies shuf'a.
2
Analyze the Maliki position on ships
Imam Malik holds that shuf'a applies to ships because ships are stationary like real estate. Understand his reasoning: the ship 'resides' (taskun) like land.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different views of the nature of ships.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis cite the hadith 'No shuf'a except in a house or a garden' and argue that shuf'a is contrary to analogy (khilaf al-qiyas), so it cannot be extended beyond real estate. Note the internal commentary addressing the apparent restriction of the hadith.
The hadith is interpreted as relative restriction, not absolute.
4
Understand buildings sold without land
When a building or palm trees are sold without the land, they are considered movable and not subject to shuf'a. If sold with the land, shuf'a applies to them as accessories.
This follows the principle that shuf'a follows the land.
5
Grasp the non-wealth consideration category
When a house is given as mahr, rent, compensation for injury, or as a gift without a condition, no shuf'a arises because the property was not acquired through a wealth-based exchange (mal). The Lawgiver only made shuf'a permissible when one owns (i.e., through a valid transfer of wealth).
The consideration must be mal (wealth) to trigger shuf'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (لا في عرض وفلك) يعني لا تجب الشفعة في عرض وفلك . وقال مالك : تجب في السفينة لأنها تسكن كالعقار. ولنا ما روي عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قال «لا شفعة إلا في ربع أو حائط ولأن الأخذ بالشفعة ثبت على خلاف القياس فلا يجوز إلحاق المنقول به لأنه ليس في معنى العقار، وهذا الاستدلال فيه شيء فإن ظاهره حصر ثبوت الشفعة في الربح والحائط فدل ذلك على انتفاء حق الشفعة في غيرهما ومن غيرهما العروض والسفن، فيرد عليه أن مقتضى الحصر أن لا تثبت الشفعة في عقار غير ربح وحائط كضيعة خالية مثلاً وليس كذلك قطعاً فكيف يتمسك به؟ قلت يمكن حمل القصر على القصر الإضافي دون الحقيقي فالقصر بالنسبة إليهما لا بالنسبة إلى جميع ما عداهما فتأمل قال في العناية : الربع الدار والحائط البستان وأصله ما أحاط به .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : ( وبناء ونخل بيعا بلا عرصة) لأنهما منقولان فلا تجب فيهما إذا بيعا بلا أرض وإن بيعا معها تجب فيها الشفعة تبعاً لها بخلاف العلو حيث يستحق بالشفعة وتستحق به الشفعة على أنه مجاوره وذلك إذا لم يكن طريقه غير طريق السفل، وإن كان طريقهما واحداً يستحق بالطريق الشفعة على أنه خليط في الحقوق. قال رحمه الله : (ودار جعلت مهراً أو أجرة أو بدل خلق أو بدل صلح عن دم عمد أو عوض عتق أو وهبت بلا عوض مشروط) لأن الشارع لم يشرع التملك بالشفعة إلا بما يملك
He (the author) said: 'Not in movable goods (arḍ) and a ship (fulk)'—meaning pre-emption does not apply to movable goods and a ship. Malik said: It applies to a ship because it is stationary like real estate. We have what was narrated from him (peace be upon him) that he said: 'No pre-emption except in a house or a garden.' And because taking by pre-emption is established contrary to analogy, so it is not permissible to annex movable property to it because it is not in the meaning of real estate. This reasoning has something: its apparent meaning confines the establishment of pre-emption to a house and a garden, indicating the negation of the right of pre-emption in other than them, and among other than them are movable goods and ships. It is objected that the implication of the confinement is that pre-emption would not be established in real estate other than a house and a garden, like an empty landed property, for example, and that is definitely not the case, so how can it be held? I say: It is possible to interpret the restriction as relative, not absolute; the restriction is with respect to them (house and garden) specifically, not with respect to everything else. Reflect. He said in al-‘Inayah: ‘Al-rub‘ (house) is a house, and al-ḥā’iṭ (garden) is a garden; its original meaning is what is enclosed.’ End quote. He (the author) said: ‘And a building and palm trees sold without a courtyard (land)’ because they are movable, so pre-emption does not apply to them if sold without land; if sold with land, pre-emption applies to them as a follow-up to the land, unlike an upper floor where it is subject to pre-emption and establishes pre-emption as a neighbor—that is when its way is not different from the lower floor; if their way is the same, he is entitled to pre-emption by way of being a partner in rights. He (the author) said: ‘And a house made as mahr, or rent, or substitute for creation (i.e., compensation for injury), or substitute for compromise for intentional homicide, or compensation for manumission, or given as a gift without a condition’ because the Lawgiver did not legislate ownership by pre-emption except by what one owns (i.e., proper consideration).
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage addresses additional cases where pre-emption does not apply. First, it confirms that shuf'a does not apply to movable goods (عرض) or ships (فلك). There is an inter-madhhab dispute with Imam Malik, who extends shuf'a to ships because ships are stationary like real estate. The Hanafi response includes a hadith: 'No shuf'a except in a house (ربع) or garden (حائط)', but the commentary notes that the hadith's apparent limitation is relative, not absolute—it means that shuf'a applies typically to these categories, but not exclusively to these two types; Hanafis extend it to all real estate. The text then discusses buildings and palm trees: when sold without land, they are movable and not subject to shuf'a; but if sold with land, they are included as accessories. An upper floor that has a separate entrance is considered a separate property for shuf'a, but if it shares an entrance with the lower floor, the owner is considered a partner in rights and qualifies for shuf'a. Finally, the passage states that if a house is given as mahr (dowry), rent, compensation for bodily harm (khilq), settlement for intentional homicide (diya), compensation for manumission (kitaba), or as a gift without a stipulated return (hiba bi-la 'iwad), then no shuf'a arises because the consideration is not wealth (mal). The pre-emptor's right is only established when the property is acquired through a valid exchange of wealth.

Key Terms
عرض (ʿarḍ): Movable goods, chattels; opposite of real estate.
فلك (fulk): Ship; considered movable property.
عرصة (ʿarṣa): Courtyard or land on which a building stands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a house given as mahr (dowry) not subject to pre-emption?
A) Because mahr is a gift and not a sale.
B) Because mahr is not considered wealth (mal) in the context of shuf'a.
C) Because the house is not real estate.
D) Because the wife cannot be forced to sell.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a is limited to real estate acquired through a wealth-based exchange; it does not extend to movable goods, ships, buildings without land, or acquisitions via mahr, rent, compensation, or gifts without condition.

Nullification of Shuf'a by Tark al-Muwathaba
Ruling with proof for nullification of shuf'a by i'rad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which shuf'a is nullified by tark al-muwathaba and explain why witnessing (ishhad) is not a condition.

Core Idea
Abandoning immediate claim (tark al-muwathaba) nullifies shuf'a because it constitutes disinterest (i'rad), not because of failure to witness.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq on the nullification of the right of pre-emption (shuf'a). The passage establishes that a shafi' who fails to claim immediately upon learning of the sale, despite being able, loses his right. This principle is linked to the concept of disinterest (i'rad). The commentator from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides an important refinement: it is the abandonment of the demand itself, not the failure to have witnesses (ishhad), that causes nullification. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of shuf'a.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by listing actions that nullify shuf'a. Your task is to identify the primary nullifying act: tark al-muwathaba (abandoning immediate claim) and its equivalent tark al-taqrir (procrastination). Note that these nullify when the shafi' knows of the sale and has the ability to act.
The ruling is stated in the matn: 'wa tabtulu bi-tark al-muwathaba aw al-taqrir.'
2
Understand the reasoning: i'rad
The commentator explains that leaving the demand while able is considered i'rad (disinterest). In Hanafi law, i'rad nullifies rights because it shows abandonment. This is the underlying cause (illa) for nullification. Recognize that i'rad is inferred from conduct, not from a verbal statement.
The text says: 'li-annahu yabtulu bi-al-i'rad wa tark al-talabayn aw ahadihima ma'a al-qudra i'rad.'
3
Distinguish tark al-talab from tark al-ishhad
The commentator critiques the expression used in al-Hidayah ('tark al-ishhad') and prefers the author's expression 'tark al-talab' (leaving the demand). Analyze why: ishhad (having witnesses) is not a condition for shuf'a; thus leaving it cannot nullify. The proper focus is on the demand itself. This step sharpens your understanding of the precise legal cause.
This is an expansion probe: the commentator refines the base text's wording.
4
Apply the basic principle of surrender
The passage states a general principle: surrender (taslim) of shuf'a before the sale is invalid, but after the right is established, dropping it is effective even without knowledge. Then it presents several scenarios from al-Muhit. Your task is to see how the principle applies: voluntary surrender (e.g., to an agent, with conditions, or to a third party) must be interpreted reasonably to determine if it is effective.
For example, saying 'I surrender it to you' to a third party is interpreted as surrender to the buyer, following the rule that speech of a sane person is interpreted for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة باب ما تبطل به الشفعة
وتبطل بترك المواثبة أو التقرير وبالصلح عن شفعته على عوض وعليه رده وبموت الشفيع لا المشتري وبيع ما يشفع به قبل القضاء بالشفعة . قال رحمه الله : ( وتبطل بترك المواثبة أو التقرير حين علم مع القدرة عليه بأن لم يمنعه أحد ولم يكن في الصلاة لأنها تبطل بالإعراض وترك الطلبين أو أحدهما مع القدرة إعراض على ما تقدم. والأصل في هذا الباب أن تسليم الشفعة قبل البيع وإن من ثبت له يصح الحق إذا أسقطه بعد ثبوته له سقط علم بثبوته له أو لم يعلم. وتعبير المؤلف بترك الطلب أولى من تعبير صاحب الهداية بترك الإشهاد لأنه يرد على صاحب الهداية أن الإشهاد ليس بشرط وترك ما ليس شرطاً في الشيء لا يبطله. وفي المحيط لو سلم الشفعة للوكيل صح وسقطت ويصح تعليق الإسقاط بشرط ولو قال سلمت لك إن اشتريت لنفسك لم تبطل إذا كان اشتراها لغيره، ولو قال لأجنبي سلمت شفعة هذا سقطت شفعته لأنه سلم مطلقاً فصرفناه إلى المشتري حملاً لكلام العاقل على الصحة. ولو قال سلمت لك لا الأجنبي بمعزل عن هذا العقد، ولو قال له أجنبي سلم للمشتري فقال سلمت لك استحساناً كأنه قال سلمت الشفعة للمشتري لشفاعتك .
Book of Pre-emption, Chapter on What Nullifies Pre-emption. It is nullified by abandoning immediate action or procrastinating, by settling for pre-emption in exchange for compensation and it is incumbent upon him to return it, by the death of the pre-emptor (not the buyer), and by selling the property for which pre-emption is sought before judgment. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And it is nullified by abandoning immediate action or procrastinating) when he knows and is able, i.e., no one prevented him and he was not in prayer, because it is nullified by disinterest, and leaving both or one of the two demands while able is disinterest, as previously discussed. The basic principle in this chapter is that surrender of pre-emption before the sale is valid, and whoever has the right, if he drops it after its establishment, it drops, whether he knows of its establishment or not. The author's expression of 'leaving the demand' is better than the author of al-Hidayah's expression of 'leaving the witnessing', because it is objected to the author of al-Hidayah that witnessing is not a condition, and leaving something that is not a condition for something does not nullify it. In al-Muhit: If he surrendered the pre-emption to the agent, it is valid and drops, and conditional dropping is valid. If he said 'I surrender it to you on condition that you buy for yourself,' it is not nullified if he bought it for someone else. If he said to a third party 'I surrender the pre-emption of this,' his pre-emption drops because he surrendered absolutely, so we refer it to the buyer, carrying the speech of a sane person to validity. If he said 'I surrender it to you' not the third party—the third party is unrelated to this contract. If a third party said to him 'Surrender to the buyer' and he said 'I surrender to you,' by istihsan it is as if he said 'I surrendered the pre-emption to the buyer through your intercession.'
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, followed by commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The ruling states that pre-emption (shuf'a) is nullified by abandoning immediate claim (tark al-muwathaba) or procrastination (tark al-taqrir). The commentator explains that this abandonment, when the shafi' is aware of the sale and able to act (not prevented, not in prayer), constitutes disinterest (i'rad) which nullifies the right. The text establishes a key principle: surrender of shuf'a before the right is established is invalid, but once established, dropping it is effective even if the shafi' was unaware of the right. A critical refinement is made regarding the author of al-Hidayah's condition of 'leaving the witnessing' (tark al-ishhad). The commentator argues that witnessing is not a condition for shuf'a, so leaving it cannot nullify; rather, it is leaving the demand (tark al-talab) that matters. The passage then presents cases from al-Muhit: surrendering to the agent is valid; conditional surrender is valid; directing surrender to a third party is interpreted as surrender to the buyer if absolute; and if a third party mediates, surrendering to that third party is considered surrender to the buyer by istihsan.

Key Terms
ترك المواثبة (tark al-muwathaba): Abandoning immediate action to claim pre-emption upon learning of the sale.
إعراض (i'rad): Disinterest or turning away from the right, which nullifies shuf'a.
تسليم الشفعة (taslim al-shuf'a): Surrendering or dropping the right of pre-emption voluntarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason that shuf'a is nullified by leaving the demand?
A) Because the shafi' fails to arrange witnesses.
B) Because it constitutes disinterest (i'rad) in the right.
C) Because the seller cancels the sale.
D) Because the property is destroyed.

Key Takeaway
Abandoning immediate claim (tark al-muwathaba) nullifies shuf'a because it manifests disinterest (i'rad); the failure to have witnesses is irrelevant.

Nullification of Shuf'a by Settlement for Compensation
Ruling with proof on settlement for compensation in shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a pre-emptor settles his right for compensation and explain why the compensation must be returned.

Core Idea
Settling shuf'a for compensation nullifies the right, but the compensation is prohibited and must be returned.

This lesson examines a crucial distinction in Hanafi pre-emption law: while the pre-emptor can validly drop his right (shuf'a) by settling with the buyer, any compensation taken for that dropping is invalid. The text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the reasoning behind this, drawing analogies to options and suretyship, and then applies the principle to various scenarios of partial settlement. Understanding this helps avoid the common pitfall of treating a mere right to acquire as a saleable property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its components
The text begins with the main ruling: if the pre-emptor settles (sulh) with the buyer for a compensation (iwad), the shuf'a is nullified but the compensation must be returned. Note that the nullification is valid but the condition for compensation is void.
The ruling has two parts: the dropping (valid) and the compensation (invalid).
2
Extract the illa (effective cause)
The reason (illa) is that the pre-emptor's right is merely a right to acquire ownership (haqq al-tamlik), not a fixed property right (mal). Therefore, taking compensation for dropping it is impermissible—it is like bribery (rishwa). The dropping is valid, but the condition for compensation is null.
The illa is the nature of the right itself.
3
Understand the analogy to khiyar al-tamlik
The text draws an analogy: if a woman with an option of divorce (khiyar al-tamlik) is told 'choose to annul for a thousand,' and she chooses, the option drops but the money is not due. Similarly, dropping shuf'a for compensation does not make the compensation due.
This qiyas reinforces the principle that dropping a mere right does not entail a property obligation.
4
Examine the counter-analogy of suretyship and the preferred view
The text compares to kafala bil nafs (suretyship for the person). In one narration, it is like shuf'a; in another, suretyship does not drop because the beneficiary did not consent. The asahh (most correct) view is that both drop without money. The commentator resolves the difference by noting that in shuf'a, there is a semantic compensation (al-iwad ma'nawi) because the price is secured for the pre-emptor, unlike suretyship.
The resolution illustrates the tarjih methodology within the Hanafi school.
5
Apply the principle to additional scenarios
The text lists several scenarios from al-Muhit and al-Jami': (1) settlement for unspecified compensation (invalid, shuf'a drops, compensation returned); (2) settlement for a known portion at a known price (valid); (3) settlement for an unknown portion (invalid, shuf'a does not drop because no disinterest); (4) third party settling (shuf'a drops but no money for third party); (5) hiring or sharecropping with knowledge of purchase (shuf'a nullified). These illustrate how the illa is applied to different fact patterns.
The key is whether the pre-emptor's conduct indicates disinterest (i'rad) in the right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وبالصلح عن شفعته على عوض وعليه رده يعني تبطل الشفعة إذا صالح المشتري الشفيع على عوض وعلى الشفيع رد العوض لأن حق الشفيع ليس بمقرر في المحل وإنما هو مجرد حق التملك فلا يجوز أخذ العوض عنه، ولا يتعلق إسقاطه بالجائز من الشرط فيما إذا قال الشفيع أسقطت شفعتي فيما اشتريت على أن تسقط حصتك فيما اشتريت أو على أن لا تطلب الثمن مني لكونه ملائماً حتى لو تراضيا سقط حق كل واحد منهما، ومع هذا لا يتعلق إسقاط الشفعة بهذا الشرط بل يسقط بمجرد قوله أسقطت تحقق الشرط أو لم يتحقق فأولى أن لا يتعلق بالشرط الفاسد وهو شرط الاعتياض عن حق ليس بمال بل هو رشوة محضة فيصح الإسقاط ويبطل الشرط، وكذا إذا باع شفعته بمال لما بينا ونظير ما نحن فيه إذا قال للمخيرة اختاري بألف أو قال ألفين لامرأته اختاري ترك الفسخ بألف فاختارت سقط الخيار ولا يثبت المال والكفالة بالنفس في هذا بمنزلة الشفعة في رواية وفي أخرى لا تبطل الكفالة ولا يجب المال. قال في شرح الجامع الكبير : إذا لم يجب العوض يجب أن لا تبطل شفعته كما في الكفالة. والفرق أن حق الشفيع قد سقط بعوض معنى فإن الثمن سلم له والمكفول له لم يرض بسقوط حقه عن الكفيل بغير عوض ولم يحصل له بعوض معنى فإن الثمن سلم له عوضاً أصلاً فلا يسقط حقه الكفالة اهـ. قال الشارح والأصح أن الكفالة والشفعة يسقطان ولا يجب المال. قيد بقوله شفعته لأنه لو صالح على أخذ نصيب الدار بنصف الثمن يجوز، ولو صالح عن بيت بحصته من الثمن لا يجوز الصلح ولا تسقط شفعته لأنه لم يوجد منه الإعراض غير أن الثمن مجهول ومثله من الجهالة يمنع صحة البيع ابتداء والأخذ بالشفعة بيع. وفي المبسوط : ساوم الشفيع المشتري أو سأله أن يوليه إياها بذلك الثمن فقال نعم فهو تسليم منه
في
«صالح عن أخذ
الشفيع لا المشتري وبيع ما يشفع به قبل القضاء بالشفعة ولا شفعة لمن باع أو بيع له
بعوض
اهـ. وفي المحيط : وهذه على ثلاثة أوجه : أحدها ما ذكره المؤلف. الثاني أن يصالح على أن يأخذ نصف الدار بنصف الثمن أو ثلث الدار بثلث الثمن فالصلح جائز لأنه أخذ معلوم بثمن معلوم. المسألة الثالثة أن يأخذ بعضها غير معلوم أو شيئاً معلوماً يبطل الصلح ولا تبطل شفعته لأن هذا لا يدل على الإعراض. وفي الجامع : صالح أجنبي أن يسلم الشفعة على مال بطلت الشفعة بلا ،مال فإن قال المصالح على أن تكون الشفعة لي لم تبطل الشفعة لأنه لم يسقط حقه بل أقام الأجنبي مقام نفسه في طلب الشفعة. وفي ابن فرشته : ولو استأجر الشفيع الدار أو أخذها منه مزارعة أو معاملة مع علمه بالشراء بطلت شفعته اهـ. والله تعالى أعلم .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And by settling for his pre-emption in exchange for compensation, and it is incumbent upon him to return it) meaning that shuf'a is nullified if the buyer settles with the pre-emptor for compensation, and the pre-emptor must return the compensation, because the right of the pre-emptor is not firmly established in the property; it is merely a right of ownership, so taking compensation for it is not permissible. And its dropping cannot be attached to a permissible condition, as when the pre-emptor says 'I have dropped my shuf'a in what you bought on condition that you drop your share in what you bought' or 'that you do not demand the price from me' because it is appropriate; even if they mutually consent, each one's right drops. Nevertheless, the dropping of shuf'a is not attached to this condition; rather it drops by his simply saying 'I dropped' whether the condition is fulfilled or not. So it is even more fitting that it should not be attached to a corrupt condition, which is the condition of taking compensation for a right that is not property, but is pure bribery. So the dropping is valid and the condition is void. Similarly, if he sold his shuf'a for money, as we have explained. An analogy to our case is when he says to a woman with the option of divorce: 'Choose for a thousand' or he says 'two thousand' to his wife: 'Choose'—the abandonment of annulment for a thousand; if she chooses, the option drops and the money is not established. And suretyship for the person in this matter is analogous to shuf'a in one narration; in another, the suretyship is not nullified nor is the money obligatory. He said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Kabir: If the compensation is not obligatory, then his shuf'a should not be nullified as in suretyship. The difference is that the right of the pre-emptor has dropped by a semantic compensation because the price is secured for him, while the beneficiary of the suretyship did not consent to the dropping of his right against the surety without compensation and no semantic compensation occurred, because the price is secured for him originally, so his right of suretyship does not drop. The commentator said: The most correct view is that both suretyship and shuf'a drop and no money is obligatory. He restricts (the ruling) to his saying 'his shuf'a' because if he settled on taking a share of the house for half the price, it is permissible; if he settled for a room for its share of the price, the settlement is not valid and his shuf'a does not drop, because no disinterest occurred from him; rather the price is unknown, and such ignorance prevents the validity of a sale initially, and taking by shuf'a is a sale. In al-Mabsut: The pre-emptor bargained with the buyer or asked him to assign it to him for that price, and he said 'yes'—that is a surrender from him. (Page 256) The pre-emptor, not the buyer, and selling what is subject to shuf'a before judgment for shuf'a—no shuf'a for one who sells or for whom it is sold for a consideration, etc. In al-Muhit: These are of three types: one is what the author mentioned; second, that he settles to take half the house for half the price or one-third for one-third the price—the settlement is valid because he takes known for known price; third, that he takes part of it unknown or something known—the settlement is invalid and his shuf'a does not drop because this does not indicate disinterest. In al-Jami': A third party settled that he would surrender the shuf'a for money—the shuf'a is nullified without any money (for the third party). If the person settling said that the shuf'a should be for him (the third party), the shuf'a is not nullified because he did not drop his right; rather he appointed the third party in his place to demand shuf'a. In Ibn Farishte: If the pre-emptor rented the house or took it on a farming or sharecropping contract (muzara'a or mu'amala) with knowledge of the purchase, his shuf'a is nullified. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with detailed proof and talil (causal reasoning). The matn states that if the pre-emptor settles (sulh) with the buyer for a consideration (iwad), the shuf'a is nullified but the consideration must be returned to the buyer. The reason (illa) is that the pre-emptor's right is merely a right to acquire ownership (haqq al-tamlik), not a settled property right (mutaqarrir fil mahall). Therefore, taking a consideration for dropping it is invalid—it is like bribery (rishwa). The dropping is valid but the condition for compensation is void. The text draws an analogy (qiyas) with the case of a woman with the option of divorce (khiyar al-tamlik) where if she chooses to annul for a price, the option drops but no money is due. A critical comparison is made with suretyship (kafala bil nafs): in one narration, it is like shuf'a (right drops, no money); in another, the suretyship does not drop because the beneficiary did not consent. The preferred view (al-asahh) is that both drop without obligation of money. The text then distinguishes between taking part of the property for a known portion (valid) versus an unknown portion (invalid, no nullification). From al-Muhit, three scenarios of settlement are given: (1) the original ruling, (2) taking a known portion for known price (valid), (3) taking an unknown portion (invalid, no nullification). Finally, hiring or taking on a farming contract with knowledge of the purchase also nullifies shuf'a per Ibn Farishte, indicating that any conduct indicating acceptance of the buyer's ownership nullifies the right.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): Settlement or compromise whereby the pre-emptor agrees to drop his right in exchange for something.
عوض (iwad): Compensation or consideration paid for dropping the right of pre-emption.
رشوة (rishwa): Bribery; the text equates taking compensation for shuf'a to bribery because it is a right, not property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pre-emptor settles with the buyer by saying 'I drop my shuf'a for 100 dirhams,' what is the ruling according to the text?
A) The shuf'a is nullified and the pre-emptor keeps the 100 dirhams.
B) The shuf'a remains, and the pre-emptor can still demand the property.
C) The shuf'a is nullified, but the pre-emptor must return the 100 dirhams.
D) The sale is invalid; the pre-emptor must pay the buyer 100 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
Settlement for compensation nullifies shuf'a but the compensation is invalid and must be returned, because the pre-emptor's right is not a property right.
