Nullification of Shuf'a by Pre-emptor's Death
Ruling that shuf'a is nullified by death of the pre-emptor but not the buyer, with Shafi'i disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that shuf'a is nullified by the pre-emptor's death before taking, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on analogy to qisas.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is a mere right of acquisition, not property, so it dies with the pre-emptor.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on whether the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) survives the death of the pre-emptor. The Hanafi position, as stated in the matn, is that it does not – it is nullified and cannot be inherited. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, analogizing shuf'a to retaliation (qisas). The Hanafi response provides a nuanced legal distinction that hinges on the nature of the right and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: shuf'a is nullified upon the death of the pre-emptor before taking, and is not inherited. Note the exception: if a judge has already awarded it or the buyer has surrendered, it becomes inheritable.
Distinguish between death before and after judicial award.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that shuf'a is not nullified, drawing an analogy to qisas (retaliation) and khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect), which are inheritable rights.
The Shafi'i view treats shuf'a as a recognized right that survives.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal distinguishes shuf'a from qisas: qisas becomes akin to owned property (mamluk) and can be compensated, while shuf'a is a mere right of ownership (haqq al-tamlik) and a mere opinion (ra'y), which cannot be compensated.
The distinction hinges on the capacity for compensation.
4
Understand the condition of ownership persistence
The pre-emptor's ownership of the property he uses to claim shuf'a must persist from the time of sale to the time of taking. Upon death, this condition fails for the deceased and the heir did not own at the time of sale, so the right is void.
This condition is specific to shuf'a and does not apply to qisas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi law, the heirs of a pre-emptor cannot claim shuf'a if he dies before taking, unless the right was already awarded. Under Shafi'i law, they can always claim. This practical difference affects inheritance planning and litigation.
The exception for judicial award is maintained in the later Hanafi tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وبموت الشفيع لا المشتري يعني بموت الشفيع قبل الأخذ بعد الطلب أو قبله تبطل الشفعة ولا تورث عنه ولا تبطل بموت المشتري. وقال الإمام الشافعي: لا تبطل بموت الشفيع أيضاً لأنه حق معتبر كالقصاص وحق الرد بالعيب. ولنا أنه مجرد حق وهو حق التمليك وأنه مجرد رأي وهو الصفقة فلا يورث عنه بخلاف القصاص لأن . من القصاص صار كالمملوك لمن له القصاص، ولهذا جاز له أخذ العوض عنده وملك العين يبقى بعد الموت فأمكن إرثه بخلاف الشفعة لأنه مجرد رأي ولهذا لا يجوز الاعتراض عنها، ولأن ملك الشفيع فيما يأخذ به الشفعة يشترط أن يكون باقياً من وقت البيع إلى وقت الأخذ بالشفعة ولم يوجد في حق الميت وقت الأخذ ولا في حق الوارث وقت البيع فبطلت لأنها لا تستحق بالملك الحادث بعد البيع ولا بالزائل بعد الأخذ، وإنما لا تبطل بموت المشتري لأن المستحق باق ولم يتغير بسبب حقه وإنما حصل الانتقال إلى الوراث فصار كما إذا انتقل إلى غيره فيأخذها . قيد لنا قبل الأخذ قال في العناية : إذا مات بعد قضاء القاضي له بالشفعة أو سلم المشتري الدار له فهي لورثته يأخذونها ولا تباع الدار في دين المشتري لأن حق الشفيع مقدم على حق المشتري، فإن باعها القاضي أو وصيه في دين الميت فللشفيع أنه ينقضه كما لو باعها المشتري في حياته، لا يقال بيع القاضي حكم منه فكيف ينقض لأنه قضاء منه مخالف للإجماع اهـ.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And by the death of the pre-emptor, not the buyer) meaning: by the death of the pre-emptor before taking, whether after demand or before, the shuf'a is nullified and is not inherited from him, and it is not nullified by the death of the buyer. Imam al-Shafi'i said: It is not nullified by the death of the pre-emptor either, because it is a recognized right like retaliation (qisas) and the right of return due to defect. For us (Hanafis), it is a mere right, which is the right of ownership, and it is a mere opinion (ra'y), i.e., the transaction, so it is not inherited unlike retaliation, because from retaliation it becomes like owned property for the one entitled to retaliation; hence he is permitted to take compensation for it in his view, and the ownership of the property remains after death, so inheritance is possible, unlike shuf'a because it is mere opinion, and therefore it is not permissible to take compensation for it. Also, the pre-emptor's ownership of the property by which he takes shuf'a must remain from the time of sale to the time of taking shuf'a, and it does not exist in the right of the deceased at the time of taking, nor in the right of the heir at the time of sale, so it is nullified, because it is not deserved by ownership acquired after the sale, nor by ownership that ceased after taking. But it is not nullified by the death of the buyer because the right-holder remains and his right does not change; rather, the property transfers to the heirs, so it is as if it transferred to someone else, so he takes it. (The ruling is) restricted to before taking. He said in al-'Inaya: If he died after the judge had decreed shuf'a for him or the buyer had surrendered the house to him, then it belongs to his heirs; they take it, and the house is not sold for the debt of the buyer because the right of the pre-emptor takes precedence over the right of the buyer. If the judge or his executor sold it for the debt of the deceased, the pre-emptor can rescind it just as if the buyer had sold it during his lifetime. It cannot be said that the judge's sale is a judicial action, so how can it be rescinded? Because it is a judgment contrary to consensus.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute (inter_madhhab_dispute) between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding whether shuf'a is nullified by the death of the pre-emptor. The Hanafi position (stated in the matn) is that it is nullified and not inheritable. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that it is not nullified, drawing an analogy to retaliation (qisas) and the right of return due to defect (khiyar al-'ayb), both of which survive death. The Hanafi response (lana) provides two arguments: First, shuf'a is a 'mere right of ownership' (haqq al-tamlik) and a 'mere opinion' (ra'y) regarding the transaction—unlike qisas, which becomes akin to owned property (mamluk) and can be compensated, shuf'a cannot be compensated and thus does not survive death. Second, the condition for shuf'a is that the pre-emptor's ownership of the property he uses to claim shuf'a must persist from the time of sale to the time of taking; in the case of the deceased, this ownership is absent at the time of taking, and the heir did not have it at the time of sale. Thus the right is void. However, the passage also clarifies that if the pre-emptor dies after the judge has awarded shuf'a or the buyer has surrendered the property, the right becomes established and is then inheritable (per al-'Inaya). The death of the buyer does not nullify shuf'a because the right-holder (shafi') remains, and the property merely passes to the buyer's heirs, so the shafi' can still claim against them.

Key Terms
حق التمليك (haqq al-tamlik): Right to acquire ownership; shuf'a entitles the pre-emptor to take ownership of the property, but this right is not property itself.
مجرد رأي (mujarrad ra'y): Mere opinion or discretion; the text describes shuf'a as based on the discretion of the pre-emptor, which does not survive death.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation (law of equality in homicide/injury); the opponent analogizes shuf'a to qisas, which is inheritable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, if the pre-emptor dies before taking the property, what happens to his right of shuf'a?
A) It passes to his heirs just like property.
B) It is nullified and cannot be claimed by heirs.
C) It remains valid if the buyer also dies.
D) It is automatically awarded to the state.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that shuf'a is nullified by the pre-emptor's death because it is a mere right of acquisition, not property, unless it has been awarded by a judge.

Nullification by Sale: The General Rule and Its Rationale
Ruling with causal reasoning (talil) on nullification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why selling the property that is the basis of shuf'a before judgment nullifies the right and state that knowledge is not a condition for nullification.

Core Idea
Selling the cause of shuf'a before judgment removes the entitlement automatically, like dropping a right—no knowledge needed.

The right of shuf'a arises from the pre-emptor's ownership of an adjacent property. If he sells that property before the judge issues a ruling, the cause of his right vanishes. This lesson examines the ruling, its reasoning, and why the pre-emptor's awareness does not affect nullification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling the property by which shuf'a is claimed before the judge's decision. The ruling (hukm) is that the shuf'a right is nullified automatically.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author provides a rational proof (talil): because the cause of entitlement has ceased before judgment.
The cause is ownership of the adjacent property.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The ownership of the adjacent property is the sabab (cause) of the shuf'a right. Selling that property removes the sabab, so the right cannot persist.
Nullification is immediate, not dependent on judgment.
4
Note the ikhtilaf on knowledge
The Hanafi position is that the pre-emptor's knowledge is irrelevant. Dropping a right (isqat) does not require awareness, analogous to divorce and emancipation.
Some schools may require knowledge.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The school relies on analogy to isqat, which is effective without acceptance or knowledge. This supports the ruling's robustness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : وبيع ما يشفع به قبل القضاء بالشفعة) يعني تبطل الشفعة الدار التي يشفع بها قبل الأخذ بالشفعة لأن سبب استحقاقه قد زال قبل القضاء بالشفعة، ولا أن يكون علماً فرق بين وقت بيع الدار بشراء المشفوعة أو لم يكن عالماً. وكذا إبراء الغريم لأن كل ذلك إسقاط فلا يتوقف على العلم كالطلاق والعتاق ألا ترى أنه لا يرتد برد المشتري، ولو باع التي يشفع بها بشرط الخيار لا تبطل شفعته ولو اشتراها الشفيع من المشتري بطلت شفعته لأنه بالإقدام على الشراء أعرض عن الشفعة، ولمن هو بعده من أو مثله أن يأخذها منه بالشفعة بالعقد الأول وإن شاء بالثاني بخلاف ما إذا اشتراها ابتداء من الشفعاء
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And selling what is shuf'a'd before judgment for shuf'a) meaning that the shuf'a is nullified regarding the house that is the basis of shuf'a before taking by shuf'a, because the cause of his entitlement has ceased before judgment for shuf'a. And knowledge does not make a difference between the time of selling the house (subject to shuf'a) and buying the (other) property subject to shuf'a, whether he was aware or not. Similarly, discharging the debtor (is like that) because all that is dropping (isqat), so it does not depend on knowledge, like divorce and emancipation. Do you not see that it (the dropping) is not revoked by the buyer's refusal? If he sold the property by which he claims shuf'a with an option (khiyar), his shuf'a is not nullified. If the pre-emptor bought it (the property subject to shuf'a) from the buyer, his shuf'a is nullified because by proceeding to purchase he has disinterested himself from shuf'a. And for those after him (i.e., other shuf'aa) or those like him, they may take it (the property) from him by shuf'a based on the first contract or, if they wish, the second contract, unlike the case where he bought it initially from the shuf'aa (i.e., the original owners).
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement (ruling_statement) with causal reasoning (talil). The matn states that selling the property by which the pre-emptor claims shuf'a before the judge delivers judgment nullifies his right. The commentator explains that the cause (sabab) of the pre-emptor's entitlement—his ownership of that property—has ceased before the judgment, so the right is lost. Importantly, this applies regardless of whether the pre-emptor knew about the sale of the property subject to shuf'a or not; dropping a right does not require knowledge, analogous to divorce and emancipation. The dropping is not reversed by the buyer's refusal. However, if the sale was with a condition of option (khiyar), the shuf'a is not nullified because the ownership does not definitively transfer. If the pre-emptor himself buys the property subject to shuf'a from the buyer, his shuf'a is nullified because his action of purchasing indicates disinterest (i'rad) in his own right of pre-emption. In that case, other pre-emptors (those with equal or inferior rank) may claim shuf'a against him based on either the original sale (from which his right arose) or the second sale (the purchase from the buyer). The exception is if he bought initially from the shuf'aa (other pre-emptors), which is not covered here.

Key Terms
إعراض (i'rad): Disinterest; the pre-emptor's act of buying the property subject to shuf'a from the buyer constitutes i'rad, nullifying his right.
شرط الخيار (shart al-khiyar): Condition of option in a sale; if the pre-emptor sells his property with an option, the sale is not final, so the cause of shuf'a remains and the right is not nullified.
إبراء الغريم (ibra' al-gharim): Discharging the debtor from a debt; analogized to selling the property as an act of isqat (dropping) that does not require knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pre-emptor sells the property he owns adjacent to the property subject to shuf'a before the judge rules, what is the effect on his shuf'a right?
A) It remains valid if he didn't know about the sale of the nearby property.
B) It is nullified regardless of his knowledge.
C) It is nullified only if the buyer also waives his right.
D) It remains valid until the option period expires.

Key Takeaway
Selling the property that is the basis of shuf'a before judgment nullifies the right automatically, regardless of the pre-emptor's knowledge.

Exceptions: Sale with Option and Pre-emptor's Purchase
Expansion of the rule with two key exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain when a sale with an option does not nullify shuf'a and when the pre-emptor's own purchase of the property constitutes disinterest (i'rad), nullifying his right, and identify the rights of other pre-emptors.

Core Idea
A sale with an option retains ownership, so shuf'a survives; but the pre-emptor's own purchase of the property shows disinterest, voiding his right.

Two important exceptions expand the general rule: if the pre-emptor sells his basis property with a condition of option, the sale is not final, so his shuf'a right remains. Conversely, if the pre-emptor himself buys the very property subject to shuf'a from the buyer, this act of purchase indicates his disinterest (i'rad) in his own right, nullifying it. However, this opens the door for other pre-emptors to take the property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Scenario 1: The pre-emptor sells his basis property with a condition of option (khiyar). Scenario 2: The pre-emptor buys the property subject to shuf'a from the buyer.
These are separate from the main rule of a final sale.
2
Analyze the option scenario
In a sale with khiyar, ownership does not transfer until the option expires or is waived. Since the pre-emptor remains the owner, the cause of shuf'a (ownership of adjacent property) persists, so his shuf'a right is not nullified.
The key is that ownership is not definitively transferred.
3
Analyze the purchase scenario
If the pre-emptor buys the subject property from the buyer, his action manifests disinterest (i'rad) in his shuf'a right. This nullifies his right, even though he still owns the adjacent property.
I'rad is an implicit waiver of the right.
4
Determine the effect on other pre-emptors
When the pre-emptor's right is nullified due to i'rad, other pre-emptors (equal or inferior in rank) may claim shuf'a against him. They can base their claim on either the original sale (from the owner to the buyer) or the second sale (the pre-emptor's purchase). Exception: if he bought initially from the original shuf'aa, different rules apply.
This ensures the right is not lost entirely; it passes to the next eligible party.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : وبيع ما يشفع به قبل القضاء بالشفعة) يعني تبطل الشفعة الدار التي يشفع بها قبل الأخذ بالشفعة لأن سبب استحقاقه قد زال قبل القضاء بالشفعة، ولا أن يكون علماً فرق بين وقت بيع الدار بشراء المشفوعة أو لم يكن عالماً. وكذا إبراء الغريم لأن كل ذلك إسقاط فلا يتوقف على العلم كالطلاق والعتاق ألا ترى أنه لا يرتد برد المشتري، ولو باع التي يشفع بها بشرط الخيار لا تبطل شفعته ولو اشتراها الشفيع من المشتري بطلت شفعته لأنه بالإقدام على الشراء أعرض عن الشفعة، ولمن هو بعده من أو مثله أن يأخذها منه بالشفعة بالعقد الأول وإن شاء بالثاني بخلاف ما إذا اشتراها ابتداء من الشفعاء
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And selling what is shuf'a'd before judgment for shuf'a) meaning that the shuf'a is nullified regarding the house that is the basis of shuf'a before taking by shuf'a, because the cause of his entitlement has ceased before judgment for shuf'a. And knowledge does not make a difference between the time of selling the house (subject to shuf'a) and buying the (other) property subject to shuf'a, whether he was aware or not. Similarly, discharging the debtor (is like that) because all that is dropping (isqat), so it does not depend on knowledge, like divorce and emancipation. Do you not see that it (the dropping) is not revoked by the buyer's refusal? If he sold the property by which he claims shuf'a with an option (khiyar), his shuf'a is not nullified. If the pre-emptor bought it (the property subject to shuf'a) from the buyer, his shuf'a is nullified because by proceeding to purchase he has disinterested himself from shuf'a. And for those after him (i.e., other shuf'aa) or those like him, they may take it (the property) from him by shuf'a based on the first contract or, if they wish, the second contract, unlike the case where he bought it initially from the shuf'aa (i.e., the original owners).
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement (ruling_statement) with causal reasoning (talil). The matn states that selling the property by which the pre-emptor claims shuf'a before the judge delivers judgment nullifies his right. The commentator explains that the cause (sabab) of the pre-emptor's entitlement—his ownership of that property—has ceased before the judgment, so the right is lost. Importantly, this applies regardless of whether the pre-emptor knew about the sale of the property subject to shuf'a or not; dropping a right does not require knowledge, analogous to divorce and emancipation. The dropping is not reversed by the buyer's refusal. However, if the sale was with a condition of option (khiyar), the shuf'a is not nullified because the ownership does not definitively transfer. If the pre-emptor himself buys the property subject to shuf'a from the buyer, his shuf'a is nullified because his action of purchasing indicates disinterest (i'rad) in his own right of pre-emption. In that case, other pre-emptors (those with equal or inferior rank) may claim shuf'a against him based on either the original sale (from which his right arose) or the second sale (the purchase from the buyer). The exception is if he bought initially from the shuf'aa (other pre-emptors), which is not covered here.

Key Terms
إعراض (i'rad): Disinterest; the pre-emptor's act of buying the property subject to shuf'a from the buyer constitutes i'rad, nullifying his right.
شرط الخيار (shart al-khiyar): Condition of option in a sale; if the pre-emptor sells his property with an option, the sale is not final, so the cause of shuf'a remains and the right is not nullified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pre-emptor sells his adjacent property with a condition of option (khiyar), what is the effect on his shuf'a right?
A) It is nullified because he sold the property.
B) It is not nullified because ownership is not transferred.
C) It is nullified only if the option is exercised.
D) It remains valid only if he does not buy the subject property.

Key Takeaway
A sale with option does not nullify shuf'a because ownership is not transferred; but if the pre-emptor himself buys the subject property, it constitutes i'rad and nullifies his right, allowing other pre-emptors to step in.

Invalidation of Pre-emption: Death, Sale, and Agreement
Rulings on invalidation of pre-emption with commentary expansions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three situations that invalidate pre-emption in the Hanafi school and apply the rulings to cases involving compromise and leasing.

Core Idea
Pre-emption is a personal right lost by death, sale of basis property, or abandonment.

Pre-emption (shuf'a) is a right for a co-owner or neighbour to step into a sale. However, this right can be invalidated by certain actions or events. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq lists the core invalidators from the matn and expands on how compromises (sulh) and leasing affect the right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three core invalidators
Read the matn: death of the pre-emptor (shafi'), sale of the property that gives the right before judgment, and the fact that the seller or one for whom the sale is made cannot claim pre-emption.
These are the primary Zāhir al-Riwāyah positions.
2
Understand the principle of non-inheritance
The first invalidator indicates that shuf'a is a personal right that does not pass to heirs upon death of the shafi'. This differs from other schools.
Shāfi‘īs hold that shuf'a is inheritable.
3
Distinguish between valid and invalid compromises
The commentary from al-Muhīt explains that if the shafi' compromises to take a known portion of the property for a known price, it is valid and does not invalidate shuf'a. However, if he takes an unknown portion or a known thing (like money) and drops his right, shuf'a is invalidated because it indicates abandonment.
Also, if a stranger pays the shafi' to surrender the right, it is invalidated; but if the stranger merely represents the shafi', it remains.
4
Recognize actions that show abandonment
Ibn Farishtah adds: leasing the property or taking it on a sharecropping/mu‘āmalah basis with knowledge of the purchase invalidates shuf'a because it implies the shafi' has accepted the situation.
These are further clarifications from the later Ḥanafī tradition.
5
Apply to scenarios
Given a factual scenario, determine whether an event falls under the invalidators. For example, does a valid compromise preserve the right? Does death of shafi' extinguish it?
Remember: the key is whether the shafi' has indicated abandonment of the right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة باب ما تبطل به الشفعة
الشفيع لا المشتري وبيع ما يشفع به قبل القضاء بالشفعة ولا شفعة لمن باع أو بيع له بعوض اهـ. وفي المحيط : وهذه على ثلاثة أوجه : أحدها ما ذكره المؤلف. الثاني أن يصالح على أن يأخذ نصف الدار بنصف الثمن أو ثلث الدار بثلث الثمن فالصلح جائز لأنه أخذ معلوم بثمن معلوم. المسألة الثالثة أن يأخذ بعضها غير معلوم أو شيئاً معلوماً يبطل الصلح ولا تبطل شفعته لأن هذا لا يدل على الإعراض. وفي الجامع : صالح أجنبي أن يسلم الشفعة على مال بطلت الشفعة بلا مال فإن قال المصالح على أن تكون الشفعة لي لم تبطل الشفعة لأنه لم يسقط حقه بل أقام الأجنبي مقام نفسه في طلب الشفعة. وفي ابن فرشته : ولو استأجر الشفيع الدار أو أخذها منه مزارعة أو معاملة مع علمه بالشراء بطلت شفعته اهـ. والله تعالى أعلم .
Chapter of Pre-emption: Section on what invalidates pre-emption. The pre-emptor (dies) not the buyer, and selling what is pre-empted with before judgment on pre-emption, and no pre-emption for one who sold or for whom sale was made with compensation. End. In al-Muhit: These are of three types: first what the author mentioned. Second, that he compromises to take half the house for half the price or one third for one third the price – the compromise is valid because he takes known for known. The third issue: that he takes some of it unspecified or something known – the compromise is invalid but his pre-emption does not invalidate because this does not indicate abandonment. In al-Jami': A stranger compromised to surrender pre-emption for money – pre-emption is invalidated without money. If he said 'the compromise is that pre-emption is for me' pre-emption is not invalidated because he did not drop his right but rather appointed the stranger as his representative in claiming pre-emption. In Ibn Farishtah: If the pre-emptor leased the house or took it on a sharecropping or mu'amalah basis with knowledge of the purchase, his pre-emption is invalidated. End. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage opens the section on invalidation of pre-emption by stating three key rulings from the matn. The commentary from al-Muhit elaborates on the second ruling concerning compromise (sulh). It distinguishes between a compromise where the pre-emptor takes a known portion for a known price (valid and does not invalidate pre-emption) and one where he takes an unknown portion or a known thing (which invalidates the compromise but does not affect pre-emption because it does not show abandonment). Al-Jami' adds that if a stranger pays the pre-emptor to surrender his right, but stipulates that the right becomes his, pre-emption is not invalidated; it is like appointing an agent. Ibn Farishtah notes that leasing or taking the property on sharecropping with knowledge of the sale invalidates pre-emption.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf‘ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbour to purchase property sold to a third party, by paying the price.
شفيع (shafī‘): The person entitled to pre-emption – the co-owner or neighbour.
صلح (sulh): Compromise or settlement – in this context, an agreement between the pre-emptor and the buyer to resolve the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pre-emptor agrees to take a known sum of money in exchange for not claiming his right of pre-emption, what is the ruling?
A) The pre-emption is invalidated because he accepted compensation.
B) The pre-emption remains valid because the compromise is invalid.
C) The pre-emption is invalidated only if the money equals the property value.
D) The pre-emption is automatically transferred to the person who paid the money.

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption is invalidated by events that show the pre-emptor has abandoned his right: death, sale of the basis property, being seller, compromise for consideration, or leasing the property with knowledge of the sale.

Death of the Pre-emptor: When the Right to Pre-emption Is Lost
Ruling and proof on death of pre-emptor vs buyer with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the death of the pre-emptor and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument by identifying the key distinction between qisas and pre-emption.

Core Idea
Pre-emption ends at the shafi's death because it is a personal right dependent on his ownership of the basis property.

A pre-emptor who dies before taking the property loses his right to pre-emption; his heirs cannot claim it. But if the buyer dies, the pre-emptor's right remains intact. Imam al-Shafi'i disagreed, arguing that pre-emption is akin to other inherited rights. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi proof and the critical distinction that preserves madhhab consistency.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Recite the ruling from the text: pre-emption is invalidated by the death of the pre-emptor (whether before or after demand but before taking) and is not inherited; it is not invalidated by the death of the buyer.
The ruling applies only if the shafi' dies before actual taking or judicial award.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that pre-emption is not invalidated by the death of the pre-emptor; it is inheritable like qisas or the right of return for defect.
Shafi'i considers it a strong right (haqq mu'tabar).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof: qisas is inheritable; the right of return for defect is inheritable; pre-emption is similar as a considered right, so it should also pass to heirs.
Qiyas is their method.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes pre-emption from qisas: qisas becomes owned (mamluk) after the victim's death, so it can be inherited; pre-emption is merely a right to take ownership (haqq al-tamlik) and depends on the shafi's continued ownership of the basis property, which ends at death. It is a mere opinion (ra'y), not a property interest.
The ownership of the basis property must persist from sale to taking; the deceased does not own at time of taking, and the heir did not own at time of sale.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, if the pre-emptor dies before taking, his heirs have no claim; in the Shafi'i school, they can step in and demand pre-emption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال عليه رحمه الله : ( وبموت الشفيع لا المشتري يعني بموت الشفيع قبل الأخذ بعد الطلب أو قبله تبطل الشفعة ولا تورث عنه ولا تبطل بموت المشتري. وقال الإمام الشافعي: لا تبطل بموت الشفيع أيضاً لأنه حق معتبر كالقصاص وحق الرد بالعيب. ولنا أنه مجرد حق وهو حق التمليك وأنه مجرد رأي وهو الصفقة فلا يورث عنه بخلاف القصاص لأن من القصاص صار كالمملوك لمن له القصاص، ولهذا جاز له أخذ العوض عنده وملك العين يبقى بعد الموت فأمكن إرثه بخلاف الشفعة لأنه مجرد رأي ولهذا لا يجوز الاعتراض عنها، ولأن ملك الشفيع فيما يأخذ به الشفعة يشترط أن يكون باقياً من وقت البيع إلى وقت الأخذ بالشفعة ولم يوجد في حق الميت وقت الأخذ ولا في حق الوارث وقت البيع فبطلت لأنها لا تستحق بالملك الحادث بعد البيع ولا بالزائل بعد الأخذ، وإنما لا تبطل بموت المشتري لأن المستحق باق ولم يتغير بسبب حقه وإنما حصل الانتقال إلى الوراث فصار كما إذا انتقل إلى غيره فيأخذها . قيد لنا قبل الأخذ قال في العناية : إذا مات بعد قضاء القاضي له بالشفعة أو سلم المشتري الدار له فهي لورثته يأخذونها ولا تباع الدار في دين المشتري لأن حق الشفيع مقدم على حق المشتري، فإن باعها القاضي أو وصيه في دين الميت فللشفيع أنه ينقضه كما لو باعها المشتري في حياته، لا يقال بيع القاضي حكم منه فكيف ينقض لأنه قضاء منه مخالف للإجماع اهـ.
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And by the death of the pre-emptor, not the buyer) meaning by the death of the pre-emptor before taking, after demand or before, pre-emption is invalidated and not inherited; and it is not invalidated by the death of the buyer. Imam al-Shafi‘i said: It is not invalidated by the death of the pre-emptor either, because it is a considered right like qisas and the right of return due to defect. Our proof: it is a mere right (haqq al-tamlik) and a mere opinion (ra'y) – the transaction – so it is not inherited, unlike qisas because part of qisas becomes like owned by the one entitled, hence he may take compensation, and property of the object remains after death so inheritance is possible, unlike shuf‘a because it is a mere opinion and therefore cannot be objected against. Also, the ownership of the pre-emptor in what he takes by shuf‘a must exist from the time of sale to the time of taking; it does not exist at the time of taking for the deceased nor at the time of sale for the heir, so it invalidates because it is not owed by new ownership after sale nor by ownership lost after taking. It is not invalidated by the death of the buyer because the entitled party remains and his right is unchanged; only transfer to the heirs occurs, so it is as if transferred to another, so the pre-emptor takes it. A condition for us is before taking. In al-‘Inayah: If he dies after the judge's judgment for him or the buyer surrenders the house to him, it is for his heirs to take; the house is not sold for the buyer's debt because the pre-emptor's right takes precedence over the buyer's right. If the judge or his executor sells it for the deceased's debt, the pre-emptor may rescind it, just as if the buyer sold it during his life. It is not said that the judge's sale is a judgment, so how can it be rescinded? Because it is a judgment contrary to consensus.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents a key ruling: pre-emption is invalidated by the death of the pre-emptor, whether he had already made a demand or not, but it is not invalidated by the death of the buyer. Imam al-Shafi‘i disagreed, arguing that pre-emption is a considered right like qisas and the right of return for a defect, and therefore should be inheritable. The Hanafi response distinguishes pre-emption from qisas: qisas becomes owned by the victim's heir after the victim's death, like property, and can be inherited. Pre-emption, on the other hand, is merely a right to take ownership (haqq al-tamlik) based on the pre-emptor's personal opinion and his existing ownership of the basis property. Since the pre-emptor's ownership of the basis property ceases at his death, and the heir did not have that ownership at the time of sale, the right cannot pass. It is like a personal option that does not survive death. However, if the pre-emptor dies after the judge's ruling or after the buyer voluntarily surrenders the property, then the right is established and passes to his heirs. The text also clarifies that the buyer's death does not affect the pre-emptor's right because the right is attached to the property, not the buyer's person.

Key Terms
حق التمليك (haqq al-tamlik): The right to take ownership – pre-emption gives the shafi' the right to compel the buyer to transfer ownership to him.
رأي (ra'y): Opinion or personal judgment – pre-emption is a mere opinion because it depends on the pre-emptor's choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does pre-emption not survive the death of the pre-emptor according to Hanafis?
A) Because the buyer's right becomes final upon the death.
B) Because pre-emption is a mere right dependent on the pre-emptor's ownership of the basis property, which ends at death.
C) Because the heirs can only claim it if the pre-emptor had already paid the price.
D) Because the property automatically belongs to the buyer after the pre-emptor's death.

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor's death before taking extinguishes the right of pre-emption in the Hanafi school; it is not inheritable, unlike the Shafi'i view.

6 min
Selling the Basis Property Invalidates Pre-emption
Ruling with proof on sale of basis property invalidating pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the actions that invalidate pre-emption (shuf'ah) and apply the principle of isqat to determine when the right is lost.

Core Idea
Selling or relinquishing the basis property before judgment drops the right of pre-emption, even without knowledge.

Pre-emption is a right to purchase a neighboring property, but it is fragile. Certain actions taken before the court awards the property can extinguish that right entirely. In this lesson, we examine the key invalidation scenarios—selling the property that gives the right, forgiving a related debt, and the effect of conditional sales—and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
The matn states that selling the property that gives the right to pre-emption (the 'basis property') before judgment invalidates the pre-emption. Understand that this is the main principle.
2
Trace the causal reasoning
The commentary explains that the cause of entitlement—ownership of the basis property—ceases upon sale. Since the right depends on continued ownership, selling removes the foundation.
The ruling applies regardless of when the basis property was sold relative to the pre-empted purchase, and regardless of the pre-emptor's knowledge.
3
Distinguish invalidating and non-invalidating actions
Forgiving a debt that gave the right also invalidates (as isqat). But selling with a condition of option (khiyar) does NOT invalidate because the sale is not final; the property might return.
Compare with buying the pre-empted property from the buyer—that invalidates because it indicates abandonment.
4
Understand the principle of isqat
Both selling the basis property and forgiving a debt are acts of relinquishment (isqat). Like divorce and manumission, isqat takes effect immediately and does not require the other party's knowledge.
The text uses analogy (qiyas) with talāq and itq to justify the ruling.
5
Apply to practical scenarios
Consider a pre-emptor who sells his adjacent house but then regrets it. Even if he repurchases it later, the right is gone. However, if he sells with khiyar, the right remains until the option expires.
Also note that other pre-emptors (e.g., equal priority) can still claim from the buyer using the original contract if the first pre-emptor bought the property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : وبيع ما يشفع به قبل القضاء بالشفعة) يعني تبطل الشفعة الدار التي يشفع بها قبل الأخذ بالشفعة لأن سبب استحقاقه قد زال قبل القضاء بالشفعة، ولا أن يكون علماً فرق بين وقت بيع الدار بشراء المشفوعة أو لم يكن عالماً. وكذا إبراء الغريم لأن كل ذلك إسقاط فلا يتوقف على العلم كالطلاق والعتاق ألا ترى أنه لا يرتد برد المشتري، ولو باع التي يشفع بها بشرط الخيار لا تبطل شفعته ولو اشتراها الشفيع من المشتري بطلت شفعته لأنه بالإقدام على الشراء أعرض عن الشفعة، ولمن هو بعده من أو مثله أن يأخذها منه بالشفعة بالعقد الأول وإن شاء بالثاني بخلاف ما إذا اشتراها ابتداء من ببيع الشفعاء
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And selling what is pre-empted with before judgment on pre-emption) means pre-emption is invalidated for the house that is pre-empted with before taking by pre-emption, because the cause of entitlement has ceased before judgment on pre-emption. There is no distinction between the time of selling the house with purchase of the pre-empted property or without knowledge. Likewise, discharging the debtor because all that is dropping (isqat) and does not depend on knowledge, like divorce and manumission. Do you not see that [the right] does not return by the rejection of the buyer? If he sells the basis property with a condition of option, his pre-emption is not invalidated. If the pre-emptor buys it from the buyer, his pre-emption is invalidated because by undertaking the purchase he abandons pre-emption. For the one after him or like him, he may take it from him by pre-emption through the first contract or, if he wishes, through the second. Unlike if he bought it initially from the seller of the pre-emptors.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This section explains the second clause of the matn: selling the property that gives the right to pre-emption (the basis property) before the shafi' takes possession invalidates the pre-emption. The reason is that the cause of entitlement (ownership of the basis property) has ceased. This applies regardless of when the basis property was sold relative to the purchase of the pre-empted property, and regardless of the shafi's knowledge. Additionally, if the shafi' forgives the debt that gave him the right (e.g., if he was a creditor), that also invalidates because it is an act of relinquishment (isqat), which does not require knowledge to take effect—like divorce or freeing a slave. The commentary also covers nuances: selling the basis property with a khiyar (option) does not invalidate pre-emption because the sale is not final; buying the pre-empted property from the buyer invalidates the right because it indicates abandonment; but other pre-emptors (e.g., those with equal priority) can still claim it from that buyer using the first sale contract, though they may also use the second if they wish. However, if the shafi' bought the property from the original seller (not from the buyer), the ruling differs.

Key Terms
إسقاط (isqāṭ): Relinquishment – acts like forgiving a debt or selling the basis property that drop the right of pre-emption without requiring notification.
شفعة (shufʿah): Pre-emption – the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property sold to an outsider, on the same terms, to prevent harm.
خيار (khiyār): Option – a condition in a contract allowing one or both parties to rescind the sale within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the shafi' (pre-emptor) sells his own property that gave him the right to pre-emption, but does so with a condition of option (khiyar), what happens to his pre-emption?
A) It is immediately invalidated.
B) It remains valid until the option period expires.
C) It remains valid because the sale is not final.
D) It is invalidated only if the buyer accepts the option.

Key Takeaway
Selling or relinquishing the basis property before the court award ends pre-emption, but a conditional sale does not have that effect.

8 min
Pre-emption: Loss of Right Due to Ratification, Guarantee, or Misleading Price
Rulings on khiyar for third party, daman al-darak, and purchase by shafi'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios in which a pre-emptor (shafi') loses his right of pre-emption and identify when he retains the right to take.

Core Idea
A pre-emptor's right can be lost by ratification, guarantee, or price deception—but a right to take may persist.

The right of pre-emption (shuf'a) is a powerful tool for a co-owner or neighbour to acquire a sold property. However, certain actions by the pre-emptor can cause him to lose that right. This lesson examines three such cases from the Hanafi text: when a third party's option is ratified, when the pre-emptor guarantees the property against defects, and when he is misled about the price. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly applying the rules of pre-emption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The central act is the pre-emptor (shafi') claiming shuf'a. The passage gives rulings on when this right is lost or retained.
The right originates from property co-ownership or adjacency.
2
Scenario 1 – Third party option ratified
If the seller stipulates an option (khiyar) for a third party and that party ratifies, the pre-emptor is considered like the seller – his ratification indicates approval, so he cannot claim pre-emption.
The pre-emptor himself may be the third party or a separate person.
3
Scenario 2 – Guarantee of darak
If the pre-emptor guarantees (ḍamān al-darak) to the seller that the property is free of defects or claims, he effectively completes the transaction, thus forfeiting pre-emption.
Darak guarantee makes the pre-emptor a party to the sale.
4
Scenario 3 – Misleading price or purchase for him
If the pre-emptor is told the price is high and agrees, then learns it was lower, he has no pre-emption – but he still has the right to take (akhdh) the property at the lower price, because his agreement did not indicate abandonment.
"Right to take" means he can still acquire the property at the real price.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
These rulings are from the Hanafi zahir al-riwayah and are the relied-upon positions. No major ikhtilaf is mentioned.
Some details may differ with other schools, but the core rules stand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شرط البائع الخيار لثالث فأجاز فهو كالبائع أو ضمن الدرك عن البائع ومن ابتاع له فله الشفعة فإن قيل للشفيع أنها بيعت بألف فسلم ثم علم أنها بيعت بأقل أو ابتيع له) بيع يعني بيع له فلا شفعة له ، فلا غير أن يثبت له فيها حق الأخذ لأن شراءها هناك لم يتضمن إعراضاً اهـ.
If the seller stipulates an option for a third party and he ratifies, it is like the seller. Or if he guarantees the darak for the seller. And whoever buys or for whom purchase is made has pre-emption. If the pre-emptor is told that it was sold for a thousand so he accedes, then learns that it was sold for less, or it was bought for him (meaning purchase for him), then he has no pre-emption, except that he has the right to take because his purchase there did not imply abandonment. End.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents several scenarios. First, if the seller stipulates an option for a third party (not the buyer) and that third party ratifies the sale, the pre-emptor is in the position of the seller (i.e., he cannot claim pre-emption because his ratification indicates approval). Second, if the shafi' guarantees the seller against defects (damān al-darak) in the property sold, his pre-emption is also lost because he has effectively completed the transaction from his side. Third, the general rule: one who buys or for whom purchase is made does have the right of pre-emption, but there are exceptions: if the pre-emptor is told the price is a thousand and he agrees, then later learns it was actually less, or if it was bought for him, then he has no pre-emption—but he maintains the right to take (akhdh) because his action (buying) did not necessarily indicate abandonment. This is nuanced: the shafi' may still have a right if he did not intend to abandon.

Key Terms
ضمان الدرك (ḍamān al-darak): Guarantee against defects or eviction – the shafi' guarantees the seller that the property is free of claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A pre-emptor guarantees to the seller that the sold property is free of defects. What is the effect on his right of pre-emption?
A) He retains the right of pre-emption.
B) He loses the right of pre-emption but can still take the property at the same price.
C) He loses the right of pre-emption and has no further claim.
D) The guarantee is void because he is not the seller.

Key Takeaway
A pre-emptor may lose shuf'a by ratification, guarantee, or price deception, but in the latter case he retains a right to take at the true price.

Selling Except a Cubit: Nullifying Pre-emption and Conditional Waiver
Sharh of matn ruling on sale except a cubit and conditional waiver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a house is sold excluding a cubit adjacent to the pre-emptor and explain why shuf'a is nullified, and distinguish valid from invalid conditional waivers of shuf'a.

Core Idea
Sale minus a cubit: no adjacency, no shuf'a — a valid legal stratagem.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq explains two related issues in shuf'a (pre-emption). First, a seller can avoid the pre-emptor's right by leaving a small strip of land unsold along the shared boundary, because shuf'a requires physical adjacency to the sold property. Second, a pre-emptor's conditional waiver ('I waive if you bought it for yourself') is only effective if the condition is fulfilled. The commentary resolves an apparent contradiction in earlier Hanafi texts.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling from the matn
Identify the explicit ruling: if a house is sold except a cubit on the side of the pre-emptor, then the pre-emptor has no shuf'a. This is the starting point.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqaiq, a core Hanafi text.
2
Identify the 'illa (effective cause) behind the ruling
Shuf'a is based on adjacency (jīwār). The excluded cubit breaks the contiguity between the sold property and the pre-emptor's property. Thus, the necessary condition for shuf'a is absent.
The 'illa is the absence of connection (ittiṣāl).
3
Construct the qiyas (analogy)
Asl: full sale with adjacency → shuf'a applies. Far': sale except adjacent strip → shuf'a does not apply because the 'illa (adjacency) is missing. This is a clear qiyas jaliy.
The ruling is derived by applying the principle that shuf'a hinges on contiguity.
4
Recognize the hila (legal stratagem)
This ruling is recognized as a permissible legal stratagem (hila) to avoid granting shuf'a. The Hanafi school allows such stratagems as long as they are not used to cause harm.
Other schools may not accept this hila, but within Hanafi it is valid.
5
Analyze the conditional waiver
If the pre-emptor says 'I waive shuf'a if you bought it for yourself' but the buyer bought for someone else, the waiver is ineffective because the condition (buying for himself) was not fulfilled. Waiver of shuf'a is like divorce: it can be conditioned and only takes effect upon fulfillment.
The commentary distinguishes conditions that indicate definite disinterest from those that are merely precautionary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذراعاً في جانب الشفيع فلا شفعة له . هذا القدر . فالتسليم في حق البعض لا يكون تسليماً في حق غيره، ولو علم أن المشتري هو مع غيره كان له أن يأخذ نصيب غيره لأن التسليم لم يوجد في حقه. قال محمد في الجامع الصغير : ولو قال الشفيع سلمت الشفعة في هذه الدار إن كنت اشتريتها لنفسك وقد اشتراها لغيره فهذا ليس بتسليم وذلك لأن الشفيع علق التسليم بشرط، هذا التعليق لأن تسليم وصح الشفعة إسقاط الحق كالطلاق فصح تعليقه بالشرط ولا يترك إلا بعد وجوده. قال صاحب العناية بعد ما نقل كلام محمد :هذا وهذا كما ترى يناقض قوله ولا يتعلق إسقاطه بالشرط الجائز فبالفاسد أولى ا هـ . وقد يجاب بأنه فرق بين شرط و شرط فما سبق كان من الشروط التي تدل على الإعراض عن الشفعة والرضا بالجوار مطلقاً، وما ذكر هنا من الشروط التي لا تدل على الإعراض ولا على الرضا فتأمل . قال رحمه الله : (وإن باعها إلا ذراعاً في جانب الشفيع فلا شفعة له) يعني إذا باع الدار إلا مقدار ذراع في طول الحد الذي يلي الشفيع فلا شفعة له لأن الاستحقاق بالجوار ولم يوجد الاتصال بالمبيع، وكذا لو وهب للمشتري لعدم الاتصال وهو حيلة
A cubit on the side of the pre-emptor: no pre-emption for him. This amount. So submission with respect to some is not submission with respect to others. If he knew that the buyer is with someone else, he may take the share of the other because submission did not occur with respect to him. Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If the pre-emptor said 'I waived pre-emption in this house if you bought it for yourself' but he bought it for someone else, this is not waiver because the pre-emptor conditioned the waiver. This conditioning is because waiver of shuf'a is a relinquishment of right like divorce, so conditioning it is valid and it is only binding after fulfillment. The author of al-`Inaya said after quoting Muhammad: 'This contradicts his statement: its relinquishment is not conditioned by a valid condition, so by an invalid one it is more so.' It may be answered that there is a difference between conditions: what preceded was conditions indicating disinterest in shuf'a and full acceptance of adjacency; what is mentioned here are conditions that do not indicate disinterest nor acceptance, so ponder. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (If he sells it except a cubit on the side of the pre-emptor, no pre-emption for him) meaning if he sells the house except a cubit in length along the boundary adjacent to the pre-emptor, no pre-emption because entitlement is by adjacency and connection to the sold does not exist. Similarly if he gifted to the buyer due to lack of connection, and this is a legal stratagem.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq. The initial lines cite three matn rulings succinctly; the sharh then comments extensively. The first issue is the condition of waiving shuf'a: if the pre-emptor says 'I waive if you bought it for yourself' but the buyer bought for another, this is not a valid waiver because it is conditional and the condition is unfulfilled. Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah notes a contradiction with the principle that relinquishment cannot be conditioned, but the response distinguishes conditions indicating disinterest from those merely precautionary. The main ruling: if the seller sells the entire property except a small strip (a cubit) along the neighbor's boundary, the neighbor has no shuf'a because the sold property does not adjoin his property. This is a recognized legal stratagem (hila) to avoid shuf'a.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase the sold property by paying its price.
حيلة (hila): A legal stratagem permissible within Sharī'ah to achieve a lawful outcome, here avoiding shuf'a.
تعليق (ta'liq): Conditioning a legal action (here, waiver of shuf'a) on the occurrence of a future event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the conditional waiver by the pre-emptor ('I waive if you bought for yourself') not nullify shuf'a when the buyer bought for someone else?
A) Because the condition is invalid and thus the waiver is void.
B) Because the condition was not fulfilled, so the waiver is not effective.
C) Because the pre-emptor did not explicitly state the waiver.
D) Because the buyer did not accept the condition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows a seller to nullify the pre-emptor's right by excluding a small adjacent strip from the sale, provided the condition of lack of adjacency is genuine, and a conditional waiver of shuf'a is only effective if the condition is fulfilled.

Legal Stratagems to Nullify Pre-emption: Before and After Obligation
Compilation of opinions on hiyal to drop shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hiyal before and after shuf'a obligation and state the Hanafi ruling for each scenario, including the intra-Hanafi difference.

Core Idea
Hiyal to drop shuf'a are permissible before obligation but makruh after – the timing of the right matters.

The Hanafi school generally permits legal stratagems (hiyal) for lawful purposes, but when it comes to pre-emption (shuf'a), the ruling depends on when the stratagem is used. This passage from several Hanafi works compiles the key opinions: after the pre-emptor's right is fixed, using a hila is disliked by consensus; before that right arises, there is a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the school's position in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of hiyal
The passage begins by classifying hiyal into two types: one that drops shuf'a after its obligation (wujub) and one that prevents its obligation from arising. Recognize that the key factor is the timing relative to the sale.
After obligation means after the sale is concluded and the pre-emptor's right is established.
2
Note the consensus after obligation
The passage states that using a hila after the pre-emptor's right is obligatory is makruh by consensus (ijma'). This applies regardless of the buyer's intention, though some earlier scholars softened this if no harm was intended.
The consensus view is that it is makruh; some sources say it is the correct (asahh) view.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute before obligation
For hiyal that prevent the obligation from arising (i.e., before the sale or before the right is fixed), the passage reports a difference: Abu Yusuf permits it, while Muhammad considers it makruh. Understand the reasoning behind each position.
Muhammad's concern: even before obligation, the intent is to deprive a neighbor of a future right. Abu Yusuf: no right is yet established, so the hila is not evasion.
4
Determine the chosen (mufta bihi) view
According to al-Khulasa and other later authorities, the chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that a hila before obligation is not disliked, whether the pre-emptor is just or sinful. This is the operative position for fatwa.
The tarjih authority is the author of al-Khulasa; later scholars like al-Haskafi and Ibn Abidin follow this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتارخانية : الحيلة في هذا الباب نوعان: نوع لإسقاطه بعد الوجوب وذلك بأن يقول للشفيع أنا أبيعها منك فقال الشفيع نعم فتبطل شفعته وهو مكروه بالإجماع ؛ كذا ذكره شيخ الإسلام وذكره شمس الأئمة أنه لا يكره إذا لم يقصد المشتري الإضرار بالشفيع وفي الينابيع : قيل الاختلاف قبل المبيع، أما بعده فمكروه بالإجماع وهو الأصح. وفي العتابية ونوع منه يمنع وجوبه وقد اختلف المشايخ قالوا على قول أبي يوسف وعلى قول محمد مكروه. وفي الذخيرة ومنهم من قال في الشفعة لا تكره الحيلة لمنع وجوبها بلا خلاف. وفي الخلاصة : الحيلة لإبطال الشفعة إن كان قبل الوجوب لا بأس به، سواء كان الشفيع عدلاً أو فاسقاً فهو المختار . وفي فتاوى الفضلي عن أبي بكر بن سعيد فقال : الحيلة بعد المبيع مكروهة في الأحوال كلها وقبل البيع إن كان الجار فاسقاً يتأذى به فلا يكره، وقيل يكره في جميع الأحوال ا هـ
And in al-TatarKhaniyya: The legal stratagems in this chapter are of two types: a type to drop it after obligation, which is that he says to the pre-emptor 'I sell it to you', so the pre-emptor says 'yes', then his shuf'a is nullified, and this is disliked (makruh) by consensus. Similarly, Sheikh al-Islam mentioned it, and Shams al-A'immah mentioned that it is not disliked if the buyer does not intend harm to the pre-emptor. And in al-Yanabi': It is said the difference is before the sale; after it, it is disliked by consensus, which is the correct view. And in al-Atabiyyah: And a type prevents its obligation, and the scholars differed: they said according to Abu Yusuf [it is permissible], and according to Muhammad it is disliked. And in al-Dhakhira: And among them is who said that in shuf'a, the stratagem to prevent its obligation is not disliked without dispute. And in al-Khulasa: The stratagem to invalidate shuf'a if before obligation is fine, whether the pre-emptor is just or sinful; that is the chosen view. And in Fatawa al-Fadhli from Abu Bakr ibn Sa'id: The stratagem after the sale is disliked in all cases; before the sale, if the neighbor is sinful and harms him, then it is not disliked; and it is said disliked in all cases end quote.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a compilation from several Hanafi works discussing the ruling on using legal stratagems (hiyal) to avoid shuf'a. The consensus is that using a hila after shuf'a becomes obligatory (i.e., after the sale is concluded and the pre-emptor's right is fixed) is disliked (makruh). However, before the obligation, there is a difference: Abu Yusuf allows it, Muhammad considers it disliked. The preferred view is that it is permissible before obligation unless harm is intended. This aligns with the Hanafi principle that hiyal are permissible for lawful purposes, but after the right is established, avoiding it is blameworthy.

Key Terms
حيلة (hila): A lawful stratagem to achieve a legal outcome otherwise blocked by a rule, here to avoid pre-emption.
وجوب (wujub): Obligation; here the moment the pre-emptor's right becomes binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does using a legal stratagem to nullify shuf'a become disliked (makruh) by consensus?
A) Before the sale is concluded.
B) After the pre-emptor's right becomes obligatory (after sale).
C) It is never disliked according to Abu Yusuf.
D) Only if the neighbor is just.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of a legal stratagem to avoid shuf'a depends entirely on timing: it is permissible before the right is fixed but makruh after, and this is the settled Hanafi position.

Pre-emption in First Share Only: Partnership Overrides Adjacency
Matn ruling with proof via qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the neighbor only has pre-emption in the first share and apply the principle that partnership takes priority over adjacency.

Core Idea
A buyer who becomes a partner through the first purchase blocks the neighbor's pre-emption in subsequent purchases.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a crucial nuance in pre-emption (shuf'a): when a buyer first purchases one share, becoming a partner, then buys the remaining shares, the neighbor's right of pre-emption is limited to the first share only. This is because partnership (shirka) creates a stronger right than mere adjacency (jiwar). The ruling is derived through qiyas and illustrates the Hanafi prioritization of rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The buyer first buys one share from a co-owner, then buys the remaining shares from other co-owners. Determine the status after the first purchase: the buyer becomes a partner.
2
Recognize the conflict
The neighbor wants to exercise pre-emption in the second purchase. Consider the competing rights: partnership vs. adjacency.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
Partnership in the property gives a stronger pre-emptive right than mere adjacency. The partner has priority over the neighbor.
This is based on the hadith 'al-shafi'u ahaqqu bi-shuf'atihi' and qiyas on co-ownership.
4
Conclude the ruling
Consequently, the neighbor only has pre-emption in the first share (where the buyer was still a stranger), not in the subsequent shares.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحمه الله : (وإن ابتاع منهما سهماً بثمن ثم ابتاع بقيمتها فالشفعة للجار في السهم الأول فقط لأن الشفيع جار في السهم الأول والمشتري شريك في السهم الثاني وهو مقدم على الجار، ولو أراد الحيلة يشتري السهم الأول بجميع الثمن إلا درهماً والسهم الثاني بدرهم فلا يرغب الجار في أخذه لكثرة الثمن، وكذا المسألة الأولى ما يأتي مثل هذه الحيلة بأن يبيع ما يلي الجار بجميع الثمن إلا درهماً ثم يشتري الباقي بدرهم فإن أخذه بالشفعة أخذ قدر الذراع بجميع الثمن وليس له أن يأخذ الباقي بدرهم، فإن أخذه بالشفعة أخذ قدر الذراع بجميع الثمن وليس له أن يأخذ الباقي لأنه ليس بجار فأيهما خاف أن لا يوفي صاحبه شرط الخيار لنفسه، وإن خافا شرط كل منهما الخيار لنفسه ثم يخيران معاً، وإن خاف كل منهما إذا أجاز لا يجيز صاحبه وكل منهما وكيلا ويشترط عليه أن يجيز بشرط أن يجيز صاحبه. وفي الفتاوى ومن جملة ذلك أن يتصدق بطبقة معينة على المشتري من الدار بطريقها ويسلمها إليه ثم يبيع الباقي منه فلا يكون للجار شفعه. وفي الخانية : أو المشتري يتصدق بمثل الثمن على البائع وهي والهبة سواء إلا أن في الهبة من الأجنبي يملك الرجوع، وفي الصدقة لا يملك الرجوع. ومنها أن جزءاً شائعاً يهب ثم يترافعا إلى حاكم يرى هبة المشاع فيما يحتمل القسمة فيحكم بجواز الهبة ثم يبيع بقية الدار منه فيكون الموهوب له مقدماً على الجار، ومن جملة ذلك أن يهب قدر ذراع من الجانب الذي هو متصل بملك الجار ا هـ
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (And if he bought from him a share for a price, then bought the remainder at its value, then the pre-emption is for the neighbor in the first share only) because the pre-emptor is neighbor in the first share, and the buyer is partner in the second share, and he is given priority over the neighbor. If he wants a stratagem, he buys the first share for the entire price except one dirham, and the second share for one dirham, so the neighbor will not desire to take it due to the high price. Similarly the first issue: such a stratagem is that he sells what adjoins the neighbor for the entire price except one dirham, then buys the remainder for one dirham; if he takes it by pre-emption, he takes the measure of a cubit for the entire price, and he may not take the remainder for one dirham because he is not a neighbor. So whichever of them fears that his companion will not fulfill, he stipulates option for himself; and if both fear, each stipulates option for himself, then they both exercise option. And if each fears that when the other approves, he will not approve, each one makes the other his agent and stipulates that he approve on condition that the other approves. And in al-Fatawa: Among that is that he gives in charity a specific segment of the house to the buyer via its path, and delivers it to him, then sells the remainder to him, so the neighbor has no pre-emption. And in al-Khaniyya: Or the buyer gives in charity the equivalent of the price to the seller, and it is the same as gift, except that in a gift from a stranger, he has the right of return, but in charity he does not have the right of return. Among them is that he gives a common share as a gift, then they go to a judge who considers gift of common property in what can be divided as valid, so he rules on the validity of the gift, then he sells the rest of the house to him, so the donee is given priority over the neighbor. Among that is that he gifts a cubit's measure from the side that is connected to the neighbor's property, end quote.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage explains the matn ruling: if a buyer first buys a share from a co-owner (making him partner), then buys the remaining shares from other co-owners, the neighbor only has pre-emption in the first share, because after the first purchase the buyer becomes a partner, and a partner has priority over a neighbor in subsequent purchases. The commentary then provides various legal stratagems to avoid pre-emption altogether, such as pricing the first share very high to discourage the neighbor, or using gifts and charity to change the status of the buyer. The inclusion of option conditions and agency shows the sophistication of Hanafi legal practice in structuring transactions to achieve desired outcomes within the law.

Key Terms
شريك (sharik): A co-owner in the property, who has a stronger pre-emption right than a neighbor.
خيار (khiyar): Option to rescind a contract, used here to devise stratagems.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the ruling of buying a share then the remainder, why does the neighbor only have pre-emption in the first share?
A) Because the first share is smaller.
B) Because the buyer becomes a partner in the second share, and partnership overrides adjacency.
C) Because the neighbor did not demand shuf'a in time.
D) Because the second share was sold at a higher price.

Key Takeaway
Partnership in the sold property gives a stronger pre-emptive right than mere adjacency, limiting the neighbor's shuf'a to the first share only.

Hiyal to Avoid Pre-emption: Stratagems in Hanafi Law
Commentary on hiyal (legal stratagems) to avoid pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct legal stratagems (hiyal) that circumvent the neighbor's pre-emption right, while understanding their conditions and validity.

Core Idea
Smart pricing, gifts, and charity can lawfully block a neighbor's pre-emption—but only if structured properly.

The Hanafi jurists not only state the law but also provide practical stratagems (hiyal) to achieve legitimate objectives within the legal framework. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq offers several methods to avoid activating the neighbor's pre-emption, from pricing tricks to using gifts and court rulings. Understanding these helps grasp the flexibility and realism of Hanafi transactional law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the goal of the hila
The goal is to prevent the neighbor from exercising pre-emption (shuf'a) on the sale. Each stratagem achieves this by changing the legal status of the buyer (e.g., making him a partner) or making the price unattractive.
2
Analyze the pricing stratagem
Buy the first share for nearly the entire price (except a token amount) and the second share for a token amount. The neighbor will not want to pay the high price for the first share, so he relinquishes his right.
3
Analyze the gift/charity stratagem
The buyer either gives a part of the property to the seller as charity or gift, or gives the seller a gift equivalent to the price. This makes the buyer a partner or extinguish the neighbor's right.
4
Analyze the court stratagem
The seller gifts a common share (even if not divided) to the buyer, then they go to a judge who holds such gifts valid. The buyer becomes a partner, and then the seller sells the rest. The buyer's partnership blocks the neighbor.
5
Recognize the use of options (khiyar) and agency
To secure the transaction, parties may stipulate options (khiyar) for rescission or appoint each other as agents to ensure mutual consent. These are ancillary tools to make the stratagem work smoothly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحمه الله : (وإن ابتاع منهما سهماً بثمن ثم ابتاع بقيمتها فالشفعة للجار في السهم الأول فقط لأن الشفيع جار في السهم الأول والمشتري شريك في السهم الثاني وهو مقدم على الجار، ولو أراد الحيلة يشتري السهم الأول بجميع الثمن إلا درهماً والسهم الثاني بدرهم فلا يرغب الجار في أخذه لكثرة الثمن، وكذا المسألة الأولى ما يأتي مثل هذه الحيلة بأن يبيع ما يلي الجار بجميع الثمن إلا درهماً ثم يشتري الباقي بدرهم فإن أخذه بالشفعة أخذ قدر الذراع بجميع الثمن وليس له أن يأخذ الباقي بدرهم، فإن أخذه بالشفعة أخذ قدر الذراع بجميع الثمن وليس له أن يأخذ الباقي لأنه ليس بجار فأيهما خاف أن لا يوفي صاحبه شرط الخيار لنفسه، وإن خافا شرط كل منهما الخيار لنفسه ثم يخيران معاً، وإن خاف كل منهما إذا أجاز لا يجيز صاحبه وكل منهما وكيلا ويشترط عليه أن يجيز بشرط أن يجيز صاحبه. وفي الفتاوى ومن جملة ذلك أن يتصدق بطبقة معينة على المشتري من الدار بطريقها ويسلمها إليه ثم يبيع الباقي منه فلا يكون للجار شفعه. وفي الخانية : أو المشتري يتصدق بمثل الثمن على البائع وهي والهبة سواء إلا أن في الهبة من الأجنبي يملك الرجوع، وفي الصدقة لا يملك الرجوع. ومنها أن جزءاً شائعاً يهب ثم يترافعا إلى حاكم يرى هبة المشاع فيما يحتمل القسمة فيحكم بجواز الهبة ثم يبيع بقية الدار منه فيكون الموهوب له مقدماً على الجار، ومن جملة ذلك أن يهب قدر ذراع من الجانب الذي هو متصل بملك الجار ا هـ
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (And if he bought from him a share for a price, then bought the remainder at its value, then the pre-emption is for the neighbor in the first share only) because the pre-emptor is neighbor in the first share, and the buyer is partner in the second share, and he is given priority over the neighbor. If he wants a stratagem, he buys the first share for the entire price except one dirham, and the second share for one dirham, so the neighbor will not desire to take it due to the high price. Similarly the first issue: such a stratagem is that he sells what adjoins the neighbor for the entire price except one dirham, then buys the remainder for one dirham; if he takes it by pre-emption, he takes the measure of a cubit for the entire price, and he may not take the remainder for one dirham because he is not a neighbor. So whichever of them fears that his companion will not fulfill, he stipulates option for himself; and if both fear, each stipulates option for himself, then they both exercise option. And if each fears that when the other approves, he will not approve, each one makes the other his agent and stipulates that he approve on condition that the other approves. And in al-Fatawa: Among that is that he gives in charity a specific segment of the house to the buyer via its path, and delivers it to him, then sells the remainder to him, so the neighbor has no pre-emption. And in al-Khaniyya: Or the buyer gives in charity the equivalent of the price to the seller, and it is the same as gift, except that in a gift from a stranger, he has the right of return, but in charity he does not have the right of return. Among them is that he gives a common share as a gift, then they go to a judge who considers gift of common property in what can be divided as valid, so he rules on the validity of the gift, then he sells the rest of the house to him, so the donee is given priority over the neighbor. Among that is that he gifts a cubit's measure from the side that is connected to the neighbor's property, end quote.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage explains the matn ruling: if a buyer first buys a share from a co-owner (making him partner), then buys the remaining shares from other co-owners, the neighbor only has pre-emption in the first share, because after the first purchase the buyer becomes a partner, and a partner has priority over a neighbor in subsequent purchases. The commentary then provides various legal stratagems to avoid pre-emption altogether, such as pricing the first share very high to discourage the neighbor, or using gifts and charity to change the status of the buyer. The inclusion of option conditions and agency shows the sophistication of Hanafi legal practice in structuring transactions to achieve desired outcomes within the law.

Key Terms
حيلة (hila): A legal stratagem or device to achieve a lawful objective without violating the law.
خيار (khiyar): Option to rescind a contract, used here to devise stratagems.
صدقة (sadaqa): Voluntary charity, which unlike a gift cannot be revoked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid stratagem to avoid pre-emption according to the passage?
A) The buyer buys the first share at a very high price and the second at a token price.
B) The buyer demands the neighbor waive his right in writing before the sale.
C) The buyer sells the property to a third party and then buys it back.
D) The buyer obtains a fatwa that pre-emption is not applicable.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law offers multiple lawful stratagems to avoid pre-emption, leveraging pricing, gifts, and legal procedures, demonstrating its practical flexibility.
